Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that power's fitness alone guarantees its action.
Correction: Fitness is not sufficient; effective connection is necessary, otherwise impotence could be implied.

Checkpoint
What does the author say is the consequence if the reason for lacking effective connection is from the essence?
A) Absolute impotence.
B) Circularity or infinite regress.
C) Nothing bad.
D) It is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Power's fitness is always present, but without effective connection, absurd consequences like circularity or impotence would follow.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's power were not effectively connected to every moment of your existence, your next breath would be impossible. Do you live with that awareness—that your very life is held by His effective power? When was the last time you thanked Him specifically for that continuous connection?
?
The passage shows that any absence of effective connection would imply either a flaw in God's essence or external constraint. When you face trials, do you ever unconsciously assume that God's power is somehow disconnected or ineffective in your situation? How can you actively affirm His perfect power even when you don't see His action?

Definition of Divine Will and Its Relation to Knowledge
Sanusi defines divine will and clarifies its dependence on knowledge and its metaphorical specification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the divine attribute of will, its role in specifying possibilities, and its dependence on knowledge in the mental order.

Core Idea
Will specifies possibilities, but it relies on knowledge.

This lesson examines Sanusi's precise definition of divine will. He describes it as an eternal attribute that specifies possible things in accordance with knowledge. The passage also clarifies that specification is ultimately attributed to God, not to will independently, and that will's operation is subordinate to knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Start by reading the Arabic and translation. Note the key terms: إرادة, تخصيص, علم.
The definition includes a footnote reference to the metaphor.
2
Understand the mental metaphor
Sanusi explains that saying 'will specifies' is a mental metaphor; the real specifier is God. Grasp this distinction.
The same applies to power: it does not truly cause.
3
Grasp the dependence on knowledge
The passage states that will's connection is a branch of knowledge—dependent and posterior in mental consideration.
This means will always follows what God knows.
4
Connect to previous lessons
Recall that power is ordered after will. Here will is ordered after knowledge, forming a rational sequence.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحقيقة الإرادة صفة أزلية يتأتى بها تخصيص (۳) الممكن ببعض ما يجوز عليه على وفق العلم (٤). (۳) وإسناد التخصيص إليها مجاز عقلي من باب الإسناد إلى السبب، وإلا فالمخصص حقيقة هو الذات الأقدس، وكذلك إسناد التأثير إلى القدرة في قول بعضهم هي صفة تؤثر في الممكن الوجود أو العدم، فهو مجاز عقلي من باب الإسناد إلى السبب وإلا فالمؤثر حقيقة هو الذات الأقدس ، إذ لا فعل إلا له كما نص عليه غير واحد من المحققين. (٤) أي إن تخصيص الله تعالى للممكن على وفق تعلق علمه تعالى به لأنه جل وعز لا يخصص بإرادته إلا ما علم من الممكنات خيراً كان أو شراً ، فكل ممكن علم الله تعالى أنه يكون أو لا يكون فذلك مراده، وبذلك تعلم أن تعلق الإرادة فرع عن تعلق العلم أي تابع له ومتأخر عنه في التعقل. And the reality of will is an eternal attribute by which is effected the specification (3) of the possible thing with some of what is permissible for it in accordance with knowledge (4). (3) The attribution of specification to it is a mental metaphor, of the category of attribution to the cause; otherwise, the one who specifies in reality is the Holy Essence. Similarly, the attribution of effect to power in the statement of some that 'it is an attribute that affects the possible thing with existence or non-existence' is a mental metaphor, of the category of attribution to the cause; otherwise, the one who affects in reality is the Holy Essence, for there is no act except His, as stated by more than one of the investigators. (4) That is, God's specification of the possible thing is in accordance with the connection of His knowledge to it, because He does not specify by His will except what He knows of possible things, be it good or evil. So every possible thing that God knows will be or will not be, that is His will. And by this you know that the connection of will is a branch of the connection of knowledge, i.e., dependent on it and posterior to it in mental consideration. (غ) And here it is said what we said previously about the ordering of power upon will and the meaning of will being posterior in mental consideration to knowledge.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the divine attribute of will (الإرادة). The definition: will is an eternal attribute by which God specifies (تخصيص) a possible thing with one of its permissible states, and this specification is in accordance with His knowledge. The author immediately adds an important qualification in the footnotes: the attribution of specification to will is a mental metaphor (مجاز عقلي); the real agent of specification is the divine essence. Similarly, attributing causal effect to power is also metaphorical—the ultimate agent is God, as there is no doer but Him. The specification is according to divine knowledge: God only wills what He knows, whether good or evil. Therefore, the connection of will is dependent on and posterior to the connection of knowledge in mental consideration (in the order of understanding), just as power is dependent on will. This is the same principle as discussed for power: the subsequence is not temporal but rational.

Misconception: Will is the independent specifier of possibilities.
Correction: Specification is metaphorically attributed to will; in reality, God alone specifies. Will is dependent on knowledge.

C) Knowledge is dependent on will.

Key Takeaway
Will is an eternal attribute that specifies possibilities, but its operation is dependent on and posterior to divine knowledge in the rational order.

Defining Divine Power (Qudra) and Will (Irada)
Definitions of qudra and irada and their logical relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of qudra and irada as eternal attributes and the logical dependency of qudra on irada in intellection.

Core Idea
Power depends on will in the mind, not in time.

In this passage, the author lays out the precise definitions of two essential divine attributes: qudra (power) and irada (will). He then explains that the attachment of power is a branch of the attachment of will, but this dependence is purely intellectual, not temporal. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the coherence of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions
Read the Arabic definitions of qudra and irada carefully. Notice the key phrases: 'eternal attribute' and 'according to will' or 'according to knowledge'.
2
Identify the dependency claim
The author states that the attachment of power is a branch of the attachment of will, posterior in intellection. Find this statement and understand what 'posterior in intellection' means.
This is not temporal posteriority.
3
Reflect on the intellectual condition
The author explains that the intellect does not perceive a powerful being unless it is also willing. This means affirming will is a necessary condition for affirming power.

وحقيقة القدرة : صفة أزلية يتأتى بها إيجاد كل ممكن وإعدامه على وفق الإرادة. وحقيقة الإرادة صفة أزلية يتأتى بها تخصيص الممكن ببعض ما يجوز عليه على وفق العلم. (٢) أي إن إيجاد الله للممكن وإعدامه له على وفق تعلق إرادته به لأنه تعالى لا يوجد أو يعدم بقدرته إلا ما أراد وجوده أو عدمه من الممكنات، وبهذا تعلم أن تعلق القدرة فرع عن تعلق الإرادة أي تابع له ومتأخر عنه في التعقل. فالتابعية هذه ليست زمانية كما قد يتوهم البعض، بل هي فقط في التعقل، بمعنى أن العقل لا يدرك قادراً إلا مريداً، فالتصديق بالإرادة شرط عقلي للتصديق بالقدرة. And the reality of Power (qudra): an eternal attribute by which is possible the bringing into existence of every possible and its annihilation in accordance with the Will (irada). And the reality of Will (irada): an eternal attribute by which is possible the specification (takhsis) of the possible with some of what is permissible for it in accordance with knowledge (ilm). (2) That is, God's bringing into existence of the possible and His annihilating it are in accordance with the attachment of His will to it, for He, exalted, does not bring into existence or annihilate by His power except what He willed its existence or non-existence from among possibles. By this you know that the attachment of power is a branch of the attachment of will, i.e., dependent on it and posterior to it in intellection (ta'aqqul). Thus this dependency is not temporal as some might imagine, but only in intellection, meaning that the intellect does not perceive a powerful being unless it is also willing, so affirming will is an intellectual condition for affirming power. (3) And the attribution of specification to it (irada) is a mental metaphor (majaz 'aqli) of the type of attribution to the cause; otherwise, the true specifier is the Most Sacred Essence. Similarly, the attribution of effect to power in the saying of some that it is an attribute that affects the possible in existence or non-existence is a mental metaphor of the type of attribution to the cause; otherwise, the true effecter is the Most Sacred Essence, for there is no act but His, as stated by more than one of the verifiers. (4) That is, God's specification of the possible is in accordance with the attachment of His knowledge to it, because He, majestic and sublime, does not specify by His will except what He knew from among possibles, whether good or evil. So every possible that God knew would be or not be, that is His will. By that you know that the attachment of will is a branch of the attachment of knowledge, i.e., dependent on it and posterior to it in intellection. And it is said here what we said previously regarding the ordering of power upon will and the meaning of will being posterior in intellection to knowledge, i.e., that the intellect cannot conceive a willing being except on condition that it is knowing, and it is not a temporal ordering as some might imagine. Because of this ordering mentioned here, the scholars say that these three attributes are ordered attributes. And know that this issue, i.e., the ordering of these three attributes as explained here, has great relevance in understanding the meaning of qada' and qadar, and upon it is resolved the objection that some might raise, who have not reflected on the meanings of creeds, that God being the creator of the actions of servants necessitates that servants are compelled in their actions. This objection is naive, i.e., not based on knowledge nor verification, and its rebuttal is based on the preceding issue as we mentioned.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage defines two divine attributes: qudra (power) and irada (will). It begins by stating the definition of qudra: an eternal attribute through which every possible being is brought into existence or non-existence according to will. Then it defines irada: an eternal attribute through which a possible is specified with some of its permissible aspects according to knowledge. Footnotes explain that 'bringing into existence' and 'causing non-existence' occur according to the attachment of His will, and that the attachment of power is a branch of the attachment of will, being subsequent in intellection only, not in time. Similarly, the attachment of will is a branch of the attachment of knowledge, subsequent in intellection. The footnote on tashbih clarifies that the attribution of specification to irada is a mental metaphor; the true specifier is the Divine Essence. The discussion then shows that these three attributes (knowledge, will, power) are ordered in intellection, which resolves the claim that human actions being created by God implies compulsion; rather, it is based on proper understanding of qada and qadar.

إرادة
تعلق
تعقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the dependence of qudra on irada implies a temporal sequence, suggesting that Allah first wills and then acquires power.
Correction: The author explicitly states that the dependence is only in intellection (fi al-ta'aqqul), not in time. Power and will are both eternal attributes; the intellect cannot conceive power without will, but they are co-eternal.

A) Qudra comes after irada in time.
B) Qudra is dependent on irada in intellection.
C) Irada is dependent on qudra in intellection.
D) There is no relationship between them.

Key Takeaway
Qudra and irada are eternal attributes, but qudra is logically dependent on irada in the mind, not in time.

The Order of Attributes and the Refutation of Compulsion
Explanation of mental metaphor and ordering of attributes, leading to resolution of compulsion objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the intellectual ordering of knowledge, will, and power, and how this resolves the objection that God's creating actions entails compulsion.

Core Idea
Knowledge, will, and power are ordered in intellect, not time, solving the qada and qadar puzzle.

The author continues by explaining that the specification of possibles is attributed to will as a mental metaphor, and that will itself depends on knowledge in intellection. This ordering of knowledge, will, and power is key to understanding divine decree (qada wa qadar) and refutes the naive objection that God's creation of human actions implies compulsion.

Guided Path
1
Understand the mental metaphor
Read the explanation that attributing specification to irada is a mental metaphor. The true specifier is the Essence itself. Similarly for qudra.
2
Trace the intellectual order
The author states that irada is a branch of knowledge in intellection. Follow the logic: the intellect cannot conceive a willing being without knowledge. This establishes an order.
Again, not temporal.
3
Apply to qada and qadar
The author concludes that this ordering resolves the compulsion objection. Reflect on how understanding the intellectual priority helps explain divine decree without compulsion.

وحقيقة القدرة : صفة أزلية يتأتى بها إيجاد كل ممكن وإعدامه على وفق الإرادة. وحقيقة الإرادة صفة أزلية يتأتى بها تخصيص الممكن ببعض ما يجوز عليه على وفق العلم. (٢) أي إن إيجاد الله للممكن وإعدامه له على وفق تعلق إرادته به لأنه تعالى لا يوجد أو يعدم بقدرته إلا ما أراد وجوده أو عدمه من الممكنات، وبهذا تعلم أن تعلق القدرة فرع عن تعلق الإرادة أي تابع له ومتأخر عنه في التعقل. فالتابعية هذه ليست زمانية كما قد يتوهم البعض، بل هي فقط في التعقل، بمعنى أن العقل لا يدرك قادراً إلا مريداً، فالتصديق بالإرادة شرط عقلي للتصديق بالقدرة. (٣) وإسناد التخصيص إليها مجاز عقلي من باب الإسناد إلى السبب، وإلا فالمخصص حقيقة هو الذات الأقدس، وكذلك إسناد التأثير إلى القدرة في قول بعضهم هي صفة تؤثر في الممكن الوجود أو العدم، فهو مجاز عقلي من باب الإسناد إلى السبب وإلا فالمؤثر حقيقة هو الذات الأقدس، إذ لا فعل إلا له كما نص عليه غير واحد من المحققين. (٤) أي إن تخصيص الله تعالى للممكن على وفق تعلق علمه تعالى به لأنه جل وعز لا يخصص بإرادته إلا ما علم من الممكنات خيراً كان أو شراً، فكل ممكن علم الله تعالى أنه يكون أو لا يكون فذلك مراده، وبذلك تعلم أن تعلق الإرادة فرع عن تعلق العلم أي تابع له ومتأخر عنه في التعقل. ويقال هنا ما قلنا سابقاً في ترتب القدرة على الإرادة ومعنى كون الإرادة متأخرة في التعقل عن العلم أي أن العقل لا يمكن أن يتعقل مريداً إلا بشرط كونه عالماً، وليس هو ترتباً زمانياً كما قد يتوهم البعض. ولهذا الترتب المذكور هنا يقول العلماء أن هذه الصفات الثلاثة صفات مترتبات واعلم أن هذه المسألة أي ترتب هذه الصفات الثلاثة كما هو مشروح هنا، لها تعلقاً كبيراً في فهم معنى القضاء والقدر، وبناءاً عليها يحل الإشكال الذي قد يوجهه البعض ممن لم يتمعن في معاني العقائد بأن كون الله تعالى خالق لأفعال العباد يلزمه أن العباد مجبورون على أفعالهم، وهذا الإيراد ساذج أي غير مبني على علم ولا على تحقيق ورده مبني على المسألة السابقة كما ذكرنا.
And the reality of Power (qudra): an eternal attribute by which is possible the bringing into existence of every possible and its annihilation in accordance with the Will (irada). And the reality of Will (irada): an eternal attribute by which is possible the specification (takhsis) of the possible with some of what is permissible for it in accordance with knowledge (ilm). (2) That is, God's bringing into existence of the possible and His annihilating it are in accordance with the attachment of His will to it, for He, exalted, does not bring into existence or annihilate by His power except what He willed its existence or non-existence from among possibles. By this you know that the attachment of power is a branch of the attachment of will, i.e., dependent on it and posterior to it in intellection (ta'aqqul). Thus this dependency is not temporal as some might imagine, but only in intellection, meaning that the intellect does not perceive a powerful being unless it is also willing, so affirming will is an intellectual condition for affirming power. (3) And the attribution of specification to it (irada) is a mental metaphor (majaz 'aqli) of the type of attribution to the cause; otherwise, the true specifier is the Most Sacred Essence. Similarly, the attribution of effect to power in the saying of some that it is an attribute that affects the possible in existence or non-existence is a mental metaphor of the type of attribution to the cause; otherwise, the true effecter is the Most Sacred Essence, for there is no act but His, as stated by more than one of the verifiers. (4) That is, God's specification of the possible is in accordance with the attachment of His knowledge to it, because He, majestic and sublime, does not specify by His will except what He knew from among possibles, whether good or evil. So every possible that God knew would be or not be, that is His will. By that you know that the attachment of will is a branch of the attachment of knowledge, i.e., dependent on it and posterior to it in intellection. And it is said here what we said previously regarding the ordering of power upon will and the meaning of will being posterior in intellection to knowledge, i.e., that the intellect cannot conceive a willing being except on condition that it is knowing, and it is not a temporal ordering as some might imagine. Because of this ordering mentioned here, the scholars say that these three attributes are ordered attributes. And know that this issue, i.e., the ordering of these three attributes as explained here, has great relevance in understanding the meaning of qada' and qadar, and upon it is resolved the objection that some might raise, who have not reflected on the meanings of creeds, that God being the creator of the actions of servants necessitates that servants are compelled in their actions. This objection is naive, i.e., not based on knowledge nor verification, and its rebuttal is based on the preceding issue as we mentioned.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage defines two divine attributes: qudra (power) and irada (will). It begins by stating the definition of qudra: an eternal attribute through which every possible being is brought into existence or non-existence according to will. Then it defines irada: an eternal attribute through which a possible is specified with some of its permissible aspects according to knowledge. Footnotes explain that 'bringing into existence' and 'causing non-existence' occur according to the attachment of His will, and that the attachment of power is a branch of the attachment of will, being subsequent in intellection only, not in time. Similarly, the attachment of will is a branch of the attachment of knowledge, subsequent in intellection. The footnote on tashbih clarifies that the attribution of specification to irada is a mental metaphor; the true specifier is the Divine Essence. The discussion then shows that these three attributes (knowledge, will, power) are ordered in intellection, which resolves the claim that human actions being created by God implies compulsion; rather, it is based on proper understanding of qada and qadar.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the ordering of knowledge, will, and power is temporal, leading to the idea that God first knows, then wills, then acts, implying change in God.
Correction: The author repeatedly says the ordering is only in intellection (fi al-ta'aqqul), not in time. All attributes are eternal; the intellect understands them in a certain order, but they are co-eternal.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for resolving the objection that God's creation of actions leads to compulsion?
A) The temporal sequence of attributes.
B) The intellectual ordering of knowledge, will, and power.
C) The denial that God creates actions.
D) The acceptance of compulsion.

Key Takeaway
The intellectual ordering of knowledge, will, and power provides the framework for understanding divine decree without falling into compulsion.

In what specific area of your life can you today surrender to this truth instead of feeling compelled by circumstances?

If the true effecter is always the Divine Essence, not the attributes independently, then what does this mean for how you approach your own actions? Are you attributing your successes or failures to secondary causes (your effort, luck, others) while forgetting the ultimate Agent? Name one situation today where you can consciously shift your attribution to God.

The lesson refutes the objection that God's creation of actions compels you. In what way have you been using the idea of 'fate' or 'God's will' to avoid personal responsibility? What is one small choice you have been postponing that you must now take ownership of, trusting that God's perfect order does not negate your agency?

Types of Attachment for Divine Will and Power
The author defines ta'luq and enumerates its types for irada and qudra.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of ta'luq (attachment) for the attributes of irada and qudra, and the two types: potential (salihi) and actual (tanjizi).

Core Idea
Divine will and power attach to possibles through a specific relation called ta'luq.

Before explaining what these attributes can and cannot attach to, the author defines the concept of attachment itself. He first lists the six pairs of contingent states, then states that qudra and irada require something additional beyond their essence, which is ta'luq. He then distinguishes between two kinds: eternal potential and eternal or temporal actual.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on six pairs
Read the Arabic text listing the six pairs of opposites that are permissible for a possible being. Notice how each pair covers a category: existence, time, place, quantity, direction, and quality.
These pairs are foundational for understanding contingency.
2
Understand the definition of ta'luq
The author says these two attributes (qudra and irada) require an extra thing beyond their subsistence in the essence. That extra thing is called attachment (ta'luq).
3
Distinguish types for irada and qudra
Irada has two attachments: saluhi qadim (potential eternal) and tanjizi qadim (actual eternal). Qudra has saluhi qadim and tanjizi hadith (actual temporal). Note that qudra's actual attachment is temporal while irada's is eternal.
This distinction reflects that will is not temporally tied to its object.
4
Prepare for the next lesson
The next lesson will explain why these attributes attach only to possibles and address an objection.

And what is permissible for the possible are the six pairs, and they are six opposites: existence, specific time, specific place, specific quantity, specific direction, and specific quality with their opposites. And these two attributes require something additional beyond their subsistence in their locus. This requirement is called attachment (ta'luq). For irada there are two attachments: potential (saluhi) eternal and actual (tanjizi) eternal. For qudra there are only two attachments: potential eternal and actual temporal. And these two attributes attach only to possibles, so they do not attach to a necessary (wajib) nor to an impossible (mumtani'), because if they attached to bringing into existence the necessary, that would entail obtaining what is already obtained, and this is rationally invalid; and if they attached to annihilating [the necessary...] (1) Al-Bayjuri said: Summary: irada has three attachments based on the view that it has a temporal actual attachment, but the investigation is that it is not an independent attachment but a manifestation of the eternal actual attachment. On this, it has only two attachments: one potential eternal and the other actual eternal. Ibn Kiraan said in his commentary on Ibn 'Ashur: Among them are those who add for irada a temporal actual attachment occurring when the predestined things appear at their times, but the truth is that the eternal actual is sufficient. And some may say with the temporal actual and the potential eternal only, so reflect. (2) It should not be said: The non-attachment of these two attributes to necessary and impossible things implies attributing inability to God due to the deficiency of His power and will from attaching to them. For we say: There is no inability and no deficiency, even if some fools among the innovators imagine otherwise. That is because inability and deficiency only follow when the power and will could have attached but did not; as for what they cannot attach to, it does not entail that at all. There is no doubt that impossible and necessary things cannot have power and will attached to them, for what you know. That is, due to the necessity of each impossible and necessary becoming possible, or the inversion of realities. (3) So the non-attachment of power to the necessary and impossible is not inability, unlike its non-attachment to the possible. As for the saying of Imam al-Ghazali: 'There is nothing in possibility more wondrous than what has been,' they have criticized it in the past because it suggests inability, which is impossible for Him. It has been answered with answers including: that what is meant by 'possibility' is the possibility of creatures, so the meaning is 'it is not within the ability of creatures to change what God has willed and created.' And among them: that what is meant is the possibility of God in terms of the attachment of His knowledge pre-eternally, and the attachment =
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage discusses the attachment (ta'luq) of the attributes of qudra and irada to possibles. It first lists the six pairs that are permissible for a possible: existence, specific time, specific place, specific quantity, specific direction, and specific quality, each with its opposite. Then it states that these two attributes require something additional beyond their subsistence in the essence, which is called attachment. For irada, there are two attachments: potential (saluhi) eternal and actual (tanjizi) eternal. For qudra, there are two attachments: potential eternal and actual temporal. Then it establishes that these attributes attach only to possibles, not to the necessary (wajib) or impossible (mumtani'), because attaching to a necessary would entail obtaining what is already obtained, which is rationally invalid. A footnote refutes the objection that this inability implies deficiency: inability only applies when the attributes could have been attached but were not; here they cannot be attached at all, so no deficiency. Another footnote discusses the debate on whether irada has a third temporal attachment, with some scholars preferring only two.

B) Two: both eternal
C) Three: one eternal and two temporal
D) One: only potential

Key Takeaway
Qudra and irada have two types of attachment: potential (eternal) and actual (eternal for irada, temporal for qudra).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every detail of your existence—whether you exist now, at this place, with this body and these qualities—is directly and specifically attached to Allah's eternal will? How does that truth confront your anxiety about the future or your regret about the past?
?
The passage teaches that Allah's power has a potential attachment that is eternal, but its actual attachment occurs only at the moment of action. Does this give you a deeper sense of reliance on Allah's timing? Are you willing to wait for His will to manifest in your life, trusting that His power is already prepared?
?

6 min
Why Divine Power and Will Attach Only to Possibles
The author proves that qudra and irada attach only to possibles and refutes the objection of inability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why qudra and irada cannot attach to the necessary or impossible, and how the objection of inability is refuted.

Core Idea
Attaching to the necessary would be redundant, and not attaching to the impossible is not a defect.

Having defined attachment, the author now establishes its scope. He argues that qudra and irada attach only to possible beings, not to the necessary or impossible. He supports this with a rational proof: attaching to the necessary would entail tahsil al-hasil (obtaining what is already obtained), which is absurd. Then he anticipates an objection: does this imply God's power is limited? He refutes it by distinguishing between inability to do what is impossible (not a defect) and failure to do what is possible.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument against attachment to necessary/impossible
Read the Arabic text starting from 'وتتعلق هاتان الصفتان بالممكنات فقط' up to the end of the objection and response. Note the logical proof based on tahsil al-hasil.
2
Understand the 'tahsil al-hasil' argument
The author says if qudra were to attach to bringing the necessary into existence, it would produce what already exists, which is rationally impossible. Similarly for annihilating the necessary. Therefore attachment is impossible.
Tahsil al-hasil is a fallacy in Aristotelian logic.
3
Analyze the objection and refutation
An objector might say this implies God is unable. The author responds that inability only applies when the object is possible; for impossible objects, the non-attachment is not failure but simply logical necessity. He calls those who think otherwise 'fools among the innovators'.
4
Reflect on the concept of divine omnipotence
Consider how this definition protects God from being attributed with trivial or contradictory acts while affirming His power over all possibles.
This lesson establishes that divine power is over all possibles, not over everything.

(2) It should not be said: The non-attachment of these two attributes to necessary and impossible things implies attributing inability to God due to the deficiency of His power and will from attaching to them. For we say: There is no inability and no deficiency, even if some fools among the innovators imagine otherwise. That is because inability and deficiency only follow when the power and will could have attached but did not; as for what they cannot attach to, it does not entail that at all. There is no doubt that impossible and necessary things cannot have power and will attached to them, for what you know. That is, due to the necessity of each impossible and necessary becoming possible, or the inversion of realities. (3) So the non-attachment of power to the necessary and impossible is not inability, unlike its non-attachment to the possible. As for the saying of Imam al-Ghazali: 'There is nothing in possibility more wondrous than what has been,' they have criticized it in the past because it suggests inability, which is impossible for Him. It has been answered with answers including: that what is meant by 'possibility' is the possibility of creatures, so the meaning is 'it is not within the ability of creatures to change what God has willed and created.' And among them: that what is meant is the possibility of God in terms of the attachment of His knowledge pre-eternally, and the attachment =
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage discusses the attachment (ta'luq) of the attributes of qudra and irada to possibles. It first lists the six pairs that are permissible for a possible: existence, specific time, specific place, specific quantity, specific direction, and specific quality, each with its opposite. Then it states that these two attributes require something additional beyond their subsistence in the essence, which is called attachment. For irada, there are two attachments: potential (saluhi) eternal and actual (tanjizi) eternal. For qudra, there are two attachments: potential eternal and actual temporal. Then it establishes that these attributes attach only to possibles, not to the necessary (wajib) or impossible (mumtani'), because attaching to a necessary would entail obtaining what is already obtained, which is rationally invalid. A footnote refutes the objection that this inability implies deficiency: inability only applies when the attributes could have been attached but were not; here they cannot be attached at all, so no deficiency. Another footnote discusses the debate on whether irada has a third temporal attachment, with some scholars preferring only two.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God's power cannot attach to impossibles, then God is not all-powerful.
Correction: Power attaches only to possible objects; impossibles are not objects of power. The inability to do what is logically impossible is not a defect.

Checkpoint

B) Because inability only applies when the power could have attached but did not; here it cannot attach at all
C) Because the necessary and impossible are not real
D) Because the author does not address the objection

Key Takeaway
Qudra and irada attach only to possibles; not attaching to necessary or impossible is not a limitation but a logical necessity.

?
Have you ever felt that God's 'inability' to answer a prayer in a particular way showed a weakness in Him? How will you now reframe that feeling in light of the distinction between impossibility and deficiency?

When you face a 'possible' in your life—something within your power to change—are you acting as if it is in your hands alone, or are you anchoring your effort in the reality that even that possibility exists only by God's qudra and irada?

The Attachments of Divine Will and Power
Sanusi defines the attachments of divine will and power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of attachment (ta'allaq) as it applies to the divine attributes of will and power, and you will be able to distinguish between the types of attachments for each attribute.

Core Idea
God's will and power have specific attachments that are either primordial or temporal.

In this passage, Sanusi introduces the technical concept of 'attachment' (ta'allaq) for the divine attributes of will and power. This concept is crucial for understanding how God's attributes relate to their objects without multiplicity in the divine essence. Let's examine the precise distinctions he makes.

Guided Path
1
Read the literal translation
Begin by reading the literal translation of the passage to get a general understanding of what Sanusi is saying about attachments.
Pay attention to the terms 'potential' and 'actualizing'.
2
Define 'attachment'
Identify how Sanusi defines attachment: as the requirement of an additional matter beyond the subsistence of the attributes in their subject.
This definition is key to understanding the rest.
3
List the attachments of will
Note that will has two attachments: both are primordial. One is potential (relating to all possibles), the other is actualizing (determining the specific moment).
Both are eternal for will.
4
List the attachments of power
Power also has two attachments: one primordial potential (like will) and one temporal actualizing (occurring only at the time of creation).
This is the key difference from will.
5
Reflect on the distinction
Consider why will's actualizing attachment is eternal while power's is temporal. This relates to the difference between willing an event and bringing it into existence.
This distinction protects divine simplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهاتان الصفتان تطلبان أمراً زائداً على قيامهما بمحلهما. وهذا الطلب يسمى تعلقا. وللإرادة تعلقان إثنان صلوحي قديم وتنجيزي قديم . والقدرة لها تعلقان إثنان فقط وهما صلوحي قديم و تنجيزي حادث.
These two attributes require an additional matter beyond their subsistence in their subject. This requirement is called 'attachment' (ta'allaq). For the will there are two attachments: a primordial potential attachment (saluhi qadim) and a primordial actualizing attachment (tanjizi qadim). For the power there are only two attachments: a primordial potential attachment (saluhi qadim) and a temporal actualizing attachment (tanjizi hadith).
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of 'attachment' (ta'allaq) for the divine attributes of will (irada) and power (qudra). It begins by stating that these two attributes require something additional beyond their mere subsistence in the divine essence. This requirement is called 'attachment', which denotes the relationship of these attributes to their objects. The passage then specifies the number and types of attachments for each: for the will, there are two attachments, both primordial (qadim): a potential attachment (saluhi) that relates to all possible things in general, and an actualizing attachment (tanjizi) that determines the exact moment of occurrence. For the power, there are also two attachments: a primordial potential attachment similar to that of the will, but only one actualizing attachment which is temporal (hadith), meaning it occurs at the time of the creation of the act. This distinction highlights the difference between will and power: the will's actualizing attachment is eternal, while the power's actualizing attachment is temporal, because power is the direct agent of bringing things into existence at their appointed times.

Correction: The passage clarifies that power has a temporal actualizing attachment while will has an eternal one.

Key Takeaway
The attachments of divine will are both eternal, while power has one eternal potential and one temporal actualizing attachment, highlighting the difference between willing and creating.

?

Proof: Divine Will and Power Attach Only to Possible Things
Sanusi proves that will and power attach only to contingent things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that divine will and power can only attach to contingent things (mumkinat), and why they cannot attach to the necessary or impossible.

Core Idea
Divine will and power are limited only by logical possibility, not by any deficiency.

Sanusi now proves an essential corollary of the previous definitions: the attributes of will and power are not omnidirectional. They apply only to the realm of possible things (mumkināt), not to the necessary or the impossible. The proof is rigorous and relies on two logical absurdities: tahṣīl al-ḥāṣil (obtaining what is already obtained) and qalb al-ḥaqā’iq (inverting realities). In this passage, you will see how these principles operate to delimit the scope of divine action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the structure: a conclusion followed by two distinct proofs.
The argument uses technical terms: wajib (necessary), mumkin (possible), mustahil (impossible).
2
Identify the two absurdities
The proof hinges on two logical fallacies: tahsil al-hasil (obtaining what is already obtained) and qalb al-haqaiq (inverting realities). Understand what each means.
Tahsil al-hasil is trying to bring about something that already exists; qalb al-haqaiq is trying to change the essential nature of a thing.
3
Analyze the proof for the Necessary
Follow the argument: If will/power attached to bringing the Necessary into existence, that would be tahsil al-hasil. If attached to annihilating it, that would be qalb al-haqaiq. Both are impossible.
The Necessary cannot cease to exist because it would then become non-necessary, a contradiction.
4
Analyze the proof for the Impossible
Similarly, for the impossible: annihilating it is tahsil al-hasil (it is already non-existent); bringing it into existence is qalb al-haqaiq (impossible cannot become existent).
The impossible does not accept existence at all.
5
Understand the conclusion
The passage concludes that will and power attach only to possible things. This is a rational necessity, not a limitation on God's power.
This aligns with the principle that divine attributes act within the realm of logical possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتتعلق هاتان الصفتان بالممكنات فقط ، فلا يتعلقان إذن بواجب ولا مستحيل ، لأنهما إن تعلقتا بإيجاد الواجب لزم تحصيل الحاصل، وهذا باطل عقلاً، وإن تعلقتا بإعدام الواجب لزم قلب الحقائق، فالواجب لا يمكن أن يفنى، لأنه إن فَنِي أصبح غير واجب. ويستحيل أن يكون الواجب غير واجب. وكذلك المستحيل إن تعلقتا بإعدامه لزم تحصيل الحاصل. وإن تعلقتا بإيجاده لزم قلب الحقائق وكلاهما باطل. فالمستحيل لا يقبل الوجود.
And these two attributes attach only to possible things (mumkinat); thus they do not attach to the necessary (wajib) nor the impossible (mustahil). Because if they attached to bringing the necessary into existence, it would entail obtaining what is already obtained (tahsil al-hasil), and this is rationally invalid. And if they attached to annihilating the necessary, it would entail inverting realities (qalb al-haqa'iq); for the necessary cannot become non-existent, because if it were to become non-existent it would become non-necessary, and it is impossible for the necessary to be non-necessary. Similarly, for the impossible: if they attached to its annihilation, it would entail obtaining what is already obtained; and if they attached to bringing it into existence, it would entail inverting realities. Both are invalid. Thus, the impossible does not accept existence.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage provides the rational proof that divine will and power attach only to contingent or possible things (mumkinat), and not to the necessary or impossible. The author begins by stating the conclusion: these two attributes attach only to possible things, so they do not attach to the necessary (wajib) or the impossible (mustahil). Then he presents the proof for each. First, regarding the necessary: if they attached to bringing the necessary into existence, that would be 'obtaining what is already obtained' (tahsil al-hasil), meaning to try to bring about something that already exists, which is inherently absurd. If they attached to annihilating the necessary, that would require inverting realities (qalb al-haqa'iq), because the necessary by definition cannot cease to exist; if it did, it would become non-necessary, which contradicts its necessity. Second, regarding the impossible: if they attached to its annihilation, that would also be obtaining what is already obtained, since the impossible is already non-existent; if they attached to bringing it into existence, that would be inverting realities, because the impossible cannot exist. Therefore, the impossible does not accept existence at all. The argument is based on logical impossibility, not on incapacity of God.

تحصيل الحاصل
قلب الحقائق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God's inability to create or annihilate the necessary or impossible indicates a deficiency in His power.
Correction: The passage clarifies that it is a matter of logical impossibility, not incapacity. God's power is unlimited, but logical contradictions are inherently impossible.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Divine will and power are limited to the realm of logical possibility, as attaching to the necessary or impossible leads to rational absurdities.

Refuting the Objection of Incapacity
Sanusi refutes the claim that God's power must extend to all things, even impossibles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the inability of divine power to attach to impossibles does not imply incapacity, and how this refutes the claim that God can take a son.

Core Idea
Impossibles are not objects of power, so their exclusion does not indicate incapacity.

Sanusi addresses a common objection: if God's power does not extend to the impossible, then God is incapable. This objection misunderstands the nature of impossibility. In this passage, Sanusi clarifies the correct criterion for incapacity and exposes the fallacy behind the claim that God can take a son. Let's read carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Begin by reading the passage once. Notice how Sanusi first states the objection, then gives the answer. The answer introduces the key criterion: incapacity only follows when power can attach but does not.
The objection is hypothetical ('It cannot be said').
2
Identify the criterion for incapacity
Sanusi distinguishes between cases where power can possibly attach and where it cannot. Incapacity applies only in the former. Understand this distinction.
This is the logical core of the argument.
3
See the application to the son claim
Sanusi applies the principle to refute those who say God can take a son. He quotes Ibn al-Arabi to show that the question is meaningless because having a son is impossible for God.
This demonstrates how the principle resolves a real controversy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال: يلزم من عدم تعلقهما بالواجبات والمستحيلات نسبة العجز إلى الله تعالى لقصور قدرته وإرادته عن التعلق بهما، لأنا نقول: لا عجز ولا قصور، وإن توهمهما بعض الأغبياء من المبتدعة، وذلك لأن العجز والقصور إنما يلزمان فيما يمكن أن تتعلق به القدرة والإرادة ولم تتعلقا به، أما ما لا يمكن أن تتعلقا فلا يلزم فيه ذلك أصلاً، ولا شك أن المستحيلات والواجبات لا يمكن تعليق القدرة والإرادة بهما لما علمت. وبهذا يعلم سقوط قول بعض المبتدعة إن الله قادر أن يتخذ ولداً، إذ لو لم يقدر عليه لكان عاجزاً. وقد رد عليه الإمام ابن العربي في كتابه العواصم من القواصم فقال: فانظروا إلى هذه الداهية العظمى كيف جهل الجائز من المستحيل في العقل والمعقول المفهوم من الكلام دون ما لا يعقل، فإن هذا الكلام ليس له معنى مفهوم إذ قوله: هل يقدر الله أن يتخذ ولدا، ليس يفهم، لأن الله هو الذي لا يتصور أن يكون له ولد، ولا يمكن، فإذن، معنى قول القائل هل يقدر الله الذي لا يصح أن يوجد منه ولد على أن يكون له ولد، فنقض آخر الكلام له فلم يكن له معنى معقول فيستحق به جوابا.
It cannot be said: From the fact that these two attributes do not attach to necessary and impossible things, there follows attribution of incapacity to God because His power and will are too deficient to attach to them. For we say: There is neither incapacity nor deficiency, even if some ignorant innovators imagine them. That is because incapacity and deficiency only follow in (cases) where power and will can possibly attach but did not attach. As for what cannot possibly attach, that does not follow at all. And there is no doubt that impossibles and necessaries cannot have power and will attached to them for what you have known. And by this it is known the falsity of the statement of some innovators that God is able to take a son, for if He were not able to do it, He would be incapable. And Imam Ibn al-Arabi replied to him in his book al-Awasim min al-Qawasim, saying: Look at this great calamity – how he was ignorant of the possible and impossible in reason, and (ignorant of) what is rationally understood from speech, as opposed to what is not rationally understood. For this statement has no intelligible meaning: when he says 'Is God able to take a son?', it is not understood, because God is the one of whom it is inconceivable to have a son, and it is not possible. Thus, the meaning of the one who says 'Is God, whose having a son is not possible, able to have a son?' is self-contradictory, and so it has no rational meaning that deserves a reply.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage refutes the objection that divine power not extending to necessary or impossible things implies incapacity. It begins by stating the objection: if God's will and power do not attach to the necessary and impossible, then God is incapable and His power is deficient. The author then gives the answer: incapacity only applies when power and will can attach to something but fail to do so; but for things that by nature cannot be objects of power and will (like necessary and impossible), the lack of attachment does not indicate incapacity. This is because necessary and impossible are not within the scope of possible objects. The passage then applies this principle to refute the claim of some innovators (Ibn Hazm is implied) who said that God can take a son, arguing that if God could not, He would be incapable. The author quotes Ibn al-Arabi's refutation: such a statement is meaningless because having a son for God is inconceivable and impossible; thus asking whether God can do an impossible thing is like asking an absurdity, which requires no answer. The argument reinforces that logical impossibility is not a limitation on divine power.

Correction: Incapacity only applies when something is possible but not done. Impossibles are not objects of power, so their exclusion does not indicate incapacity.

Divine Power and Foreknowledge: Al-Ghazali's Best of All Possible Worlds
Sanusi states the principle of executive power and defends al-Ghazali against al-Biqa'i.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how divine executive power aligns with foreknowledge and how al-Ghazali's statement about the best possible world was defended against the objection of attributing incapacity to God.

Core Idea
Divine power acts only in accordance with foreknowledge; otherwise knowledge would become ignorance.

Sanusi begins by stating a fundamental principle: executive power always conforms to what God knows. He then addresses a famous objection by al-Biqa'i to al-Ghazali's statement that nothing in possibility is more perfect than what exists. The defense shows that al-Ghazali did not imply incapacity; rather, God's power and will are simply not attached to creating a more perfect world, though He could if He willed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how Sanusi moves from a general principle to a specific objection and answer.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the principle of executive power
Find the sentence about executive power (al-qudra al-tanjiziyya). What does it say about its relationship with divine knowledge?
3
Analyze the objection and response
Al-Biqa'i objected that al-Ghazali's statement implies incapacity. How does the response clarify the meaning? Why does it not imply incapacity?
Focus on the phrase 'not attached to its bringing into existence'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القدرة التنجيزي لا يكون إلا على طبق ما سبق به العلم، وإلا لانقلب العلم جهلا. ومنها أن المراد ليس في الإمكان جعل الحادث قديماً. اعترض البقاعي على الغزالي في قوله : ليس في الإمكان أبدع مما كان بأن فيه نسبة العجز إليه تعالى. لكن أجيب بأن المراد أنه لا يمكن أن يوجد أبدع من هذا العالم لعدم تعلق قدرة الله وإرادته بإيجاده. ولو شاء الله تعالى لأوجد أبدع منه، فليس في كلامه ما يقتضي نسبة العجز إليه تعالى كما توهمه البقاعي فاعترض عليه. ولا يلزم من عدم تعلق القدرة بهما عجز لأنهما ليسا من وظيفتها ولأنها لو تعلقت بهما لزم الفساد إذ يلزم عليه تعلقهما بإعدام الذات العلية، وبسلب الألوهية عنها ونحو ذلك، وبهذا يعلم سقوط قول بعض المبتدعة إن الله قادر أن يتخذ ولداً، إذ لو لم يقدر عليه لكان عاجزاً.
The executive power is only in accordance with what has preceded in knowledge, otherwise knowledge would turn into ignorance. Among [the points] is that what is meant is that it is not possible to make the originated eternal. Al-Biqa'i objected to al-Ghazali's statement: 'There is nothing in possibility more perfect than what has been' by saying that it implies attributing incapacity to Him. But it was answered: The meaning is that nothing more perfect than this world can exist because God's power and will are not attached to its bringing into existence. And if God had willed, He would have created something more perfect, so there is nothing in his words that implies attributing incapacity to Him as al-Biqa'i imagined, so he objected. And it does not follow from the non-attachment of power to them that there is incapacity, because they are not within its function, and because if it were attached to them it would entail corruption, for it would entail attachment to the annihilation of the exalted Essence, and to stripping divinity from It, and the like. And by this, the saying of some innovators is known to be false that 'God is able to take a son', because if He were not able, He would be incapable.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a theological discussion on the nature of divine power. It first asserts that executive power (al-qudra al-tanjiziyya) always aligns with foreknowledge; otherwise knowledge would become ignorance. It then addresses al-Biqa'i's objection to al-Ghazali's famous statement about the best possible world. The response clarifies that al-Ghazali did not mean God is incapable, but that no more perfect world exists because God's will and power are not attached to it; if He willed, He could create a more perfect one. The passage then elaborates on why power not attaching to impossibilities does not imply incapacity: impossibilities are not within the function (wazifa) of power. If power were attached to impossibilities, it would lead to absurdities like annihilating God's essence or stripping His divinity. Thus, the innovated claim that God can take a son is refuted, because that would imply that if He were not able, He would be incapable—but in reality, such an act is impossible and not an object of power.

العلم
الإمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Ghazali's statement 'There is nothing in possibility more perfect than what has been' implies that God is incapable of creating a better world.
Correction: The passage clarifies that it means no more perfect world actually exists because God's will and power are not attached to it, not that God is unable.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the non-existence of a more perfect world not imply God's incapacity?
A) Because God's power is limited to the actual world.
B) Because God's power and will are not attached to creating a more perfect world.
C) Because al-Ghazali was mistaken.
D) Because the world is already perfect.

Key Takeaway
Executive power always matches foreknowledge, and al-Ghazali's statement does not imply God's incapacity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

When something bad happens, do you ever think it should not have happened, as if you know better than God? How does this lesson challenge you to surrender that doubt?
?
Al-Ghazali's statement was misunderstood. Are there any beliefs you hold about God that might be based on misunderstanding rather than revelation? What would it take for you to correct them?
?
If God could have created a more perfect world, but chose this one, what does that say about your own complaints about the world? Are you willing to trust that His choice is best, even when you don't understand?

Divine Power and Impossibilities: Refuting the Claim That God Can Have a Son
Sanusi explains why power's non-attachment to impossibilities is not incapacity, and refutes a deviant claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's power not attaching to impossibilities does not imply incapacity, and how this refutes the innovated claim that God can take a son.

Core Idea
Impossibilities are not objects of power; denying power over them is not incapacity.

The passage continues by explaining that if power does not attach to something, it does not mean God is incapable. Impossibilities like annihilating God's essence or taking a son are not within the function of power. This addresses a common innovation: that God could take a son. Understanding this protects tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Focus on the logical argument: why non-attachment of power is not incapacity.
2
Understand the function of power
The passage says impossibilities are not within the function (wazifa) of power. What does this mean? Why would attaching power to them lead to corruption?
Think about examples: annihilating God's essence or stripping divinity.
3
Apply to the innovated claim
The innovated claim is that God can take a son. How does the principle refute this? Why would denying this power not be incapacity?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القدرة التنجيزي لا يكون إلا على طبق ما سبق به العلم، وإلا لانقلب العلم جهلا. ومنها أن المراد ليس في الإمكان جعل الحادث قديماً. اعترض البقاعي على الغزالي في قوله : ليس في الإمكان أبدع مما كان بأن فيه نسبة العجز إليه تعالى. لكن أجيب بأن المراد أنه لا يمكن أن يوجد أبدع من هذا العالم لعدم تعلق قدرة الله وإرادته بإيجاده. ولو شاء الله تعالى لأوجد أبدع منه، فليس في كلامه ما يقتضي نسبة العجز إليه تعالى كما توهمه البقاعي فاعترض عليه. ولا يلزم من عدم تعلق القدرة بهما عجز لأنهما ليسا من وظيفتها ولأنها لو تعلقت بهما لزم الفساد إذ يلزم عليه تعلقهما بإعدام الذات العلية، وبسلب الألوهية عنها ونحو ذلك، وبهذا يعلم سقوط قول بعض المبتدعة إن الله قادر أن يتخذ ولداً، إذ لو لم يقدر عليه لكان عاجزاً.
The executive power is only in accordance with what has preceded in knowledge, otherwise knowledge would turn into ignorance. Among [the points] is that what is meant is that it is not possible to make the originated eternal. Al-Biqa'i objected to al-Ghazali's statement: 'There is nothing in possibility more perfect than what has been' by saying that it implies attributing incapacity to Him. But it was answered: The meaning is that nothing more perfect than this world can exist because God's power and will are not attached to its bringing into existence. And if God had willed, He would have created something more perfect, so there is nothing in his words that implies attributing incapacity to Him as al-Biqa'i imagined, so he objected. And it does not follow from the non-attachment of power to them that there is incapacity, because they are not within its function, and because if it were attached to them it would entail corruption, for it would entail attachment to the annihilation of the exalted Essence, and to stripping divinity from It, and the like. And by this, the saying of some innovators is known to be false that 'God is able to take a son', because if He were not able, He would be incapable.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a theological discussion on the nature of divine power. It first asserts that executive power (al-qudra al-tanjiziyya) always aligns with foreknowledge; otherwise knowledge would become ignorance. It then addresses al-Biqa'i's objection to al-Ghazali's famous statement about the best possible world. The response clarifies that al-Ghazali did not mean God is incapable, but that no more perfect world exists because God's will and power are not attached to it; if He willed, He could create a more perfect one. The passage then elaborates on why power not attaching to impossibilities does not imply incapacity: impossibilities are not within the function (wazifa) of power. If power were attached to impossibilities, it would lead to absurdities like annihilating God's essence or stripping His divinity. Thus, the innovated claim that God can take a son is refuted, because that would imply that if He were not able, He would be incapable—but in reality, such an act is impossible and not an object of power.

Key Terms
العجز
الذات العلية
الألوهية
مبتدعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God can take a son; if He cannot, then He is incapable.
Correction: Taking a son is impossible (entails absurdities), and power does not attach to impossibilities. Therefore, denying it is not a sign of incapacity.

Checkpoint
Why does the non-attachment of God's power to impossibilities not imply incapacity?
A) Because God chooses not to use His power.
B) Because impossibilities are not within the function of power.
C) Because God's power is limited.
D) Because God is not required to have power over everything.

Key Takeaway
God's power does not extend to impossibilities, and this does not diminish His perfection.

How can you live out this acceptance in your daily life, especially when faced with questions about God's attributes?

Rationale Behind Proper Expressions of Divine Power
Sanusi critiques al-Bayjuri's fatwa and explains why 'power does not attach' is preferable to 'God is not able'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sunni theologians avoid saying 'God is not able' regarding impossibilities and instead say 'power does not attach,' and why this distinction matters for theological precision.

Core Idea
The way we speak about God's power must avoid any implication of deficiency.

In this passage, Sanusi critiques a fatwa that allowed saying 'God cannot remove me from His kingdom.' He argues that the phrase 'God is not able' is misleading because it can imply that God has power but is unable, suggesting deficiency. The correct expression is that God's power does not attach to the impossible, because the impossibility lies in the object, not in God's ability. This lesson will unpack his reasoning and the theological principle behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the full translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the way Sanusi structures his critique: he quotes al-Bayjuri, then gives his own view, then supports it.
2
Identify the two competing expressions
Find the two key phrases: 'لا يقدر الله' (God is not able) and 'لا تتعلق قدرة الله' (God's power does not attach). Understand why Sanusi says the first is problematic.
The issue is that 'لا يقدر' could imply God has power but is unable, which suggests deficiency.
3
Understand the mosquito wing analogy
Sanusi uses the example of a human being unable to create a mosquito's wing. He explains two possible meanings: one implies deficiency (the person is prevented), the other implies the act is beyond his capacity altogether. Apply this to God.
The second meaning is appropriate for God, because the impossible is simply not an object of power.
4
Note the supporting citations
Sanusi quotes al-Shahrastani and al-Tafjarruti to reinforce his position. See how they also prohibit the phrase 'God is not able' and prescribe 'power does not attach.'
These citations show that Sanusi's view is not idiosyncratic but part of a broader scholarly consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونقل البيجوري عن بعضهم أنه سئل عمن قال : لا يقدر الله أن يخرجني من مملكته هل يكفر أو لا ؟ فأجاب بأنه لا يكفر لأن خروجه من مملكته تعالى مستحيل، لعدم إمكان وجود مملكة لغيره فلا يخرجه إليها، والقدرة لا تتعلق بالمستحيل فلا ضير في ذلك كما لا ضير في أن يقال لا يقدر الله أن يتخذ ولداً أو زوجة أو نحو ذلك . أهـ كلامه، أقول: وهذا الجواب ضعيف، ولو قال البيجوري " لا تتعلق قدرة الله بالمستحيل" لكان أحْسَنَ، لأن قولك الله لا يقدر يوهم النقص لأنه يلزم منه أن القدرة أصلاً تتعلق ولكنه لا يقدر، والأصل في الجواب أن يقال أصل القدرة لا تتعلق بالمستحيل، لأن المستحيل لا يقبل الوجود أصلا. وفرق بين أن يقال لا يقدر وبين أن يقال القدرة لا تتعلق بذلك وبيانه كما يلي : إذا قلنا إن الإنسان لا يقدر على أن يخلق جناح بعوضة فهناك احتمالان لمعنى هذه العبارة : الأول : أن الإنسان يصلح لأن يخلق، ولكن هناك مانع منعه من خلق المذكور بعينه مع. الثاني : أن الإنسان لا يصلح لأن يخلق شيئاً أصلاً ولذلك فلا يستطيع خلق جناح بعوضة وظاهر أن المعنى الأول يلزم منه نقص قدرته وعجزه قطعاً، بخلاف الثاني لاحتماله عدم وجود قدرته على الخلق أصلا وفرق كبير بين المعنيين، وكذلك قول القائل الله لا يقدر على المستحيل، يحتمل هذين المعنيين، ولكن قولنا قدرة الله لا تتعلق أصلاً بالمستحيل، فهذا المعنى يستلزم فقط عدم صلوحية المستحيل للانفعال بالقدرة، لأن فاعلية القدرة واحدة، فالنقص جاء من جهة المستحيل لا من جهة القدرة وهذا المعنى لا يستلزم أي وجه من وجوه النقص، ولعدم تنبه البعض لهذا الفرق ظنَّ منعنا من القول الأول مبنيا على وجوب الأدب في حق الله فقط، مع أنه ليس كذلك ملاحظة أن الافتاء يتألف في هذه الحالة من قسمين، الأول : الحكم بأن القائل هل يكفر أولا، والثاني علة هذا الحكم وانتقادي إنما توجه على التعليل لا على الحكم، بل أقول إن القائل بعدم قدرة الله على ذلك إذا ظن أنه مما يصح تعلق القدرة به فقد يكفر، وإلا فتعبيره غلط لإجماله كما قلنا، ومعنى الإجمال هنا هو عدم تمامية الجواب كما ينبغي لمقام الافتاء فكان اللازم التنبيه لاحتمال العبارة محل السؤال معنى باطلاً، وبناءً على ذلك يضبط الجواب، والله اعلم. فتأمل ويَشْهَدُ لما حَرَّرتُه ما قاله الإمام الشهرستاني في نهاية الإقدام ص ٤٧ : " وأما ما يستحيل وجوده، فلا يقال : الباري تعالى ليس قادرا عليه بل يقال : المستحيل في ذاته غير مقدور، فلا يتصور وجوده".اهـ. ثم وَجَدْت نصاً آخر لأحد علماء المغرب وهو الشيخ أحمد بن محمد التفجروتي من علماء القرن العاشر الهجري، قال في كتابه تنبيه الغافل عما يظنه عالم وهو به جاهل : " فإن قيل أيضا هل يجوز أن يقال : الله لا يقدر على المحال والله غير قادر به؟ قلنا : لا يجوز لما في اللفظ من إيهام التعجيز، ولا يجوز إطلاق الألفاظ الموهمة في حق الله تعالى إلا بتوقيف والصواب أن يقال : المحال لا تتعلق به القدرة، أو المحال غير مقدور، وشبه ذلك مما لا يتوهم نقصاً في حقه سبحانه وتعالى. هذا ما ذكره أئمتنا - رضي الله عنهم - وهو بين" اهـ. فظهر بهذا صواب ما قرّرناه سابقا، والحمد الله.
Al-Bayjuri reported from someone that he was asked about one who said: 'God cannot remove me from His kingdom' – does he become a disbeliever or not? He answered: He does not become a disbeliever, because his departure from His kingdom is impossible, since there is no possibility of a kingdom belonging to other than Him, so He does not remove him to it, and power does not attach to the impossible, so there is no harm in that, just as there is no harm in saying: God cannot take a son or a wife or the like. End of his words. I say: This answer is weak. If al-Bayjuri had said: 'God's power does not attach to the impossible,' it would have been better, because your saying 'God cannot' implies deficiency, because it implies that power does attach in principle but He is not able. The proper answer is to say: Power does not attach to the impossible in principle, because the impossible does not accept existence at all. There is a difference between saying 'He is not able' and saying 'power does not attach to that'. Its clarification is as follows: If we say that a human being is not able to create a mosquito's wing, there are two possible meanings: (1) the human is fit to create but there is an obstacle preventing that specific creation; (2) the human is not fit to create anything at all, and therefore cannot create a mosquito's wing. Evidently the first meaning necessarily implies deficiency in his power and incapacity, unlike the second, because it implies the absence of his power to create at all. There is a great difference between the two meanings. Similarly, the saying of the speaker 'God is not able to do the impossible' can imply these two meanings. But our saying 'God's power does not attach at all to the impossible' entails only the impossibility of the impossible being affected by power, because the effectiveness of power is one; the deficiency comes from the side of the impossible, not from the side of power. This meaning does not entail any aspect of deficiency. Because some did not realize this difference, they thought that our prohibiting the first expression is based only on the necessity of respect toward God, but it is not so. Note that fatwa in this case consists of two parts: (1) the ruling on whether the speaker becomes a disbeliever or not, and (2) the reason for this ruling. My criticism is directed at the reasoning, not at the ruling. Rather I say: The one who denies God's power over that, if he thinks that it is something valid for power to attach, he may become a disbeliever; otherwise his expression is wrong due to its ambiguity, as we said. The meaning of ambiguity here is that the answer is not complete as it should be for the position of fatwa. So one must draw attention to the possibility of a false meaning in the expression in question, and based on that the answer is determined. And God knows best. Consider, and what Imam al-Shahrastani said in Nihayat al-Iqdam (p. 47) bears witness to what I have written: 'As for that whose existence is impossible, one should not say: The Creator is not able to do it, but rather one should say: The impossible in itself is not power-able, so its existence is not conceivable.' End. Then I found another text from a scholar of the Maghrib, Shaykh Ahmad ibn Muhammad al-Tafjarruti from the 10th century AH, who said in his book Tanbih al-Ghafil 'amma yazunnuhu 'alim wa huwa bihi jahil: 'If it is also asked: Is it permissible to say: "God is not able to do the impossible, and God is not able to do it?" We say: It is not permissible, because of the implication of attributing incapacity in the wording. And it is not permissible to utter words that imply deficiency concerning God except with a textual warrant. The correct thing is to say: The impossible does not attach to it power, or the impossible is not empowered, and the like, which does not imply deficiency concerning Him.' End. So the correctness of what we have previously established is apparent. Praise be to God.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of a fatwa by al-Bayjuri. The author argues that saying 'God cannot do such-and-such' (e.g., 'God cannot remove me from His kingdom') is problematic because the wording 'cannot' (la yaqdir) could imply that God has power in principle but is unable in this case, which suggests deficiency. The better expression is 'power does not attach to it' (la tata'allaq al-qudra bihi), because the deficiency lies in the object (the impossible) not in God. The author illustrates with the analogy of a human not being able to create a mosquito's wing, showing two possible meanings: one implying the human has power but is prevented (indicating deficiency), the other implying the human is not capable of such an act at all (which does not necessarily indicate deficiency if the act is impossible). For God, since impossibilities are not within the scope of power, the correct formulation attributes the impossibility to the object, not to God's power. The author then supports his position with citations from al-Shahrastani and al-Tafjarruti, who also prohibit the phrase 'God is not able' because it implies ta'jiz (attributing incapacity). The correct phrasing is 'the impossible is not an object of power' or 'power does not attach to it'. Finally, the author notes that a fatwa should be precise, especially in avoiding ambiguous wording that might lead to false beliefs.

Key Terms
لا يقدر
لا تتعلق القدرة
المستحيل
الإيهام
التعجيز
التوقيف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that saying 'God cannot' is merely a matter of politeness and that it is acceptable as long as the meaning is understood.
Correction: The passage shows that the phrase 'God cannot' can imply that God has power but is unable, which is a theological error because it suggests deficiency in divine power. The correct expression must avoid any such implication by stating that power does not attach to the impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is saying 'God's power does not attach to the impossible' better than saying 'God is not able to do the impossible'?
A) Because it is shorter.
B) Because 'not able' might imply that God has power but is unable, suggesting deficiency.
C) Because 'not able' is grammatically incorrect.
D) Because the impossible is not an object of power at all.

Key Takeaway
To avoid any hint of deficiency in divine power, one must say that power does not attach to the impossible, not that God is unable to do it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Proof for Allah's Power and Will from Revelation and Consensus
Sanusi cites Qur'an, Sunna, and consensus as proofs for qudra and irada.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the attributes of power and will are proven primarily through revelation and consensus, with rational proof following.

Core Idea
Revelation and consensus come before reason in establishing divine attributes.

Before delving into rational arguments, Sanusi grounds the attributes of power and will in the Qur'an, Sunna, and consensus. This passage shows that the foundational proofs for these essential attributes are scriptural and communal, preparing the student for later rational proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic original and the English translation side by side. Notice how each proof is introduced.
The passage is concise; each sentence presents a distinct proof.
2
Identify the two attributes and their proofs
Find where power (qudra) and will (irada) are each proven. For each attribute, note the three types of proof: Qur'an, Sunna, and consensus.
The consensus is mentioned after both attributes are individually proven.
3
Understand the order of proofs
Observe that Sanusi first gives the Book, then the Sunna, then consensus. Ask why he mentions rational proof only at the end, and what that implies about its priority.
This reinforces reliance on revelation before independent reason.
4
Reflect on the role of consensus
Consider why the consensus of Ahl al-Sunna is cited as a separate proof. How does it strengthen the evidence?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليل على وجوب وصف الله تعالى بالقدرة من الكتاب قوله تعالى: {إِنَّ اللهَ عَلَى كُلِّ شَيْءٍ قَدِيرٌ} [البقرة: 148]، لأن حقيقة القادر من له قدرة. ومن السنة عد الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم في أسماء الله تعالى اسم القادر. ودليل الإرادة من الكتاب قوله تعالى: {فَعَّالٌ لِمَا يُرِيدُ} [البروج: 16]، ومن السنة قوله: (2) "ما شاء الله كان وما شاء لم يكن". وأجمع أهل السنة على أن الله تعالى متصف بالقدرة والإرادة وأما برهان العقل فسيأتي.
The proof for the obligation of describing Allah Almighty with power from the Book is His saying: 'Indeed, Allah is over all things competent' (al-Baqara 2:148), because the reality of the Powerful (al-Qadir) is one who has power. And from the Sunna is the enumeration by the Messenger (peace be upon him) among the names of Allah Almighty of the name al-Qadir. And the proof for will from the Book is His saying: 'Doer of what He wills' (al-Buruj 85:16), and from the Sunna is his saying: (2) 'What Allah wills occurs and what He does not will does not occur.' And the people of the Sunna have consensus that Allah Almighty is described with power and will. As for the rational proof, it will come later.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage provides the scriptural and consensus-based proofs for the necessity of describing Allah with power (qudra) and will (irada). It begins by citing the Qur'anic verse 'Indeed, Allah is over all things competent' as evidence for power, explaining that the reality of being powerful (qadir) is to possess power (qudra). Then it mentions that the Prophet included 'al-Qadir' among the names of Allah in the Sunna. Similarly, for will, the verse 'Doer of what He wills' and the prophetic saying 'What Allah wills occurs...' are cited. Finally, it notes that the people of the Sunna (Ahl al-Sunna) have consensus (ijma') on Allah being described with power and will, and that the rational proof will follow later. This establishes that before engaging in rational theology, the foundational attributes are affirmed through revelation and consensus.

Key Terms
القدرة
الإرادة
الدليل
الإجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think rational proof alone suffices for proving divine attributes.
Correction: This passage shows that revelation and consensus are foundational; rational proof is supplementary and comes later.

Checkpoint

B) Power and will
C) Will and hearing
D) Knowledge and sight

Key Takeaway
Allah's power and will are established through Qur'an, Sunna, and consensus before any rational argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you face a situation beyond your control, do you truly believe that Allah is over all things competent? How does that belief affect your anxiety or plans? This week, commit to reciting 'Indeed, Allah is over all things competent' daily and examine your heart for any doubt.

The Prophet taught us to say 'What Allah wills occurs, and what He does not will does not occur.' In what area of your life do you struggle to accept that Allah's will is absolute? What would change if you truly submitted to that in your heart? Make a specific intention to repeat this hadith whenever you feel resistance.
?
Consensus of the scholars affirms Allah's power and will. Do you realize that rejecting or doubting these attributes contradicts the collective agreement of the Muslim community? How seriously do you take ijma' in your personal beliefs? Ask yourself: am I willing to stand apart from Ahl al-Sunna on matters they have unanimously affirmed?
