The Seven Attributes of States
Sanusi enumerates and defines the seven attributes of states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven attributes of states (al-ṣifāt al-ma‘nawiyyah) and how each is a necessary state (ḥāl) resulting from a corresponding attribute of meaning rooted in the divine essence.

Core Idea
The seven states are necessary concomitants of the seven attributes of meaning.

In this passage, Sanusi enumerates the seven attributes of states (al-ṣifāt al-ma‘nawiyyah) that necessarily inhere in the divine essence. Each is defined as a state (ḥāl) that follows from the presence of a corresponding attribute of meaning—power, will, knowledge, life, hearing, sight, and speech. Understanding these states helps clarify the relationship between Allah's essential attributes and His actual descriptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the pattern
Notice that each attribute is introduced with 'kawnuhu' (His being) and explained as a necessary state due to the subsistence of the corresponding attribute.
2
Study the definitions
For each of the seven, read the definition of the reality (ḥaqīqah) of that state. Note how each is grounded in a specific attribute of meaning.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why these states are considered necessary and how they relate to the divine essence without being separate eternal entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والصفات المعنوية هي
كونه قادراً : والكون هو الثبوت وحقيقة القادر هو المتمكن من الفعل والترك. كونه مريدا وهي حال واجبة للذات لما قامت الإرادة بذات الله تعالى وجب وصفه بكونه مريداً. وحقيقة المريد هو الذي يرجح أحد طرفي الممكن على الطرف الآخر. كونه عالماً : هي حال واجب للذات ، لما قام العلم بذات الله تعالى وجب وصفه بكونه وحقيقة العالم هو الذي انكشف لعلمه الواجب والمستحيل والجائز. كونه حياً : هي حال واجبة للذات. لما قامت الحياة بذات الله وجب وصفه بكونه حياً. وحقيقة الحي هو الذي صح وصفه بصفات الإدراك.
كونه سميعاً : هي حال واجبة للذات، لما قام السمع بذات الله وجب وصفه بكونه سميعا. وحقيقة السميع هو الذي انكشف لسمعه كل موجود.
كونه بصيراً : هي حال واجبة للذات، لما قام البصر بذات الله وجب وصفه بكونه بصيرا، وحقيقة البصر هو الذي انكشف البصره كل موجود.
كونه متكلماً : هي حال واجبة للذات ، لما قام الكلام بذات الله وجب وصفه بكونه متكلماً، وحقيقة المتكلم الذي دل كلامه على الواجب والمستحيل والجائز.
And the attributes of states are: His being powerful (kawnuhu qādiran): and al-kawn is existence (al-thubūt), and the reality of the powerful (al-qādir) is the one who is capable (al-mutamakkin) of action and inaction. His being willing (kawnuhu murīdan): it is a state (ḥāl) necessary for the essence; since will (al-irāda) subsists in the essence of Allah the Exalted, it is necessary to describe Him as being willing. And the reality of the willing (al-murīd) is the one who gives preponderance (yurajjiḥ) to one of the two sides of a possible thing (al-mumkin) over the other side. His being knowing (kawnuhu ‘āliman): it is a state necessary for the essence; since knowledge (al-‘ilm) subsists in the essence of Allah the Exalted, it is necessary to describe Him as being knowing (kawnuhu ‘āliman). And the reality of the knowing (al-‘ālim) is the one whose knowledge reveals (inkasha‘a) the necessary, the impossible, and the possible. His being living (kawnuhu ḥayyan): it is a state necessary for the essence; since life (al-ḥayāh) subsists in the essence of Allah, it is necessary to describe Him as being living. And the reality of the living (al-ḥayy) is the one who is correctly described with the attributes of perception (al-idrāk). His being hearing (kawnuhu samī‘an): it is a state necessary for the essence; since hearing (al-sam‘) subsists in the essence of Allah, it is necessary to describe Him as being hearing. And the reality of the hearing (al-samī‘) is the one whose hearing reveals every existing thing. His being seeing (kawnuhu baṣīran): it is a state necessary for the essence; since sight (al-baṣar) subsists in the essence of Allah, it is necessary to describe Him as being seeing. And the reality of the seeing (al-baṣīr) is the one whose sight reveals every existing thing. His being speaking (kawnuhu mutakalliman): it is a state necessary for the essence; since speech (al-kalām) subsists in the essence of Allah, it is necessary to describe Him as being speaking. And the reality of the speaking (al-mutakallim) is the one whose speech indicates (dalla) the necessary, the impossible, and the possible.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage enumerates and defines the seven attributes of states (al-ṣifāt al-ma‘nawiyyah). Each is introduced with the phrase 'kawnuhu...' (His being...), indicating that it is a state (ḥāl) necessarily subsisting in the essence due to the corresponding attribute of meaning. The first is His being powerful (kawnuhu qādiran): 'al-kawn' means existence (thubūt), and the reality of 'powerful' is one who has the ability (tamakkun) to act or refrain from acting. The second is His being willing (kawnuhu murīdan): a state necessitated by will (irāda) subsisting in His essence; the willing is the one who gives preponderance to one of two possible outcomes. The third is His being knowing (kawnuhu ‘āliman): necessitated by knowledge (‘ilm) subsisting in His essence; the knowing is one whose knowledge unveils the necessary (wājib), impossible (mustaḥīl), and possible (jā’iz). The fourth is His being living (kawnuhu ḥayyan): necessitated by life (ḥayāh) subsisting in His essence; the living is one validly described with attributes of perception (idrāk). The fifth is His being hearing (kawnuhu samī‘an): necessitated by hearing (sam‘); the hearing is one whose hearing reveals every existent. The sixth is His being seeing (kawnuhu baṣīran): necessitated by sight (baṣar); the seeing is one whose sight reveals every existent. The seventh is His being speaking (kawnuhu mutakalliman): necessitated by speech (kalām); the speaking is one whose speech indicates the necessary, impossible, and possible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These seven states are independent eternal attributes existing alongside the attributes of meaning.
Correction: They are not independent; they are necessary concomitant states (aḥwāl) that necessarily follow from the subsistence of the attributes of meaning in the essence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the reality of 'the hearing' (al-samī‘)?
A) The one who hears sounds.
B) The one whose hearing reveals every existing thing.
C) The one who has ears.
D) The one who can speak.

Key Takeaway
The seven attributes of states are necessary concomitants of the seven attributes of meaning, and each is defined by its unique reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that Allah's hearing (sam‘) reveals every existing thing. Every word you have ever spoken, every thought you have ever entertained, is fully exposed to Him. Is there a conversation you are planning that you would be ashamed for Him to hear? Will you cancel it, or will you speak as if He is not listening?
?
The reality of Allah's power (qudra) is that He is truly capable of action and inaction—nothing constrains Him. You, on the other hand, are constrained by your abilities, your resources, your very body. When you fail to achieve something you desperately want, do you rage against God as if He owes you success? Or do you submit to His absolute power, acknowledging that every outcome is His alone to give?

Definition of the Seven Moral Attributes
Al-Sanusi enumerates the seven moral attributes and defines each one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what the seven moral attributes are, why they are called 'moral', and how each is defined in relation to its corresponding attribute of meaning.

Core Idea
The seven moral attributes are necessary states that arise from the seven attributes of meaning.

Al-Sanusi now introduces the seven moral attributes, which are the states (أحوال) that necessarily accompany the seven attributes of meaning. Before diving into each, he explains why they are called 'معنوية' (moral) — because they are attributed to the meanings (المعاني) and are inseparable from them. This passage gives a concise definition of each moral attribute, linking it to its corresponding attribute of meaning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the term 'معنوية'
Read the opening sentence explaining why they are called moral attributes. Note that the inseparability is mutual.
2
Notice the pattern
Each moral attribute follows the same structure: 'كونه [attribute]: it is a state necessary for the Essence when [corresponding attribute] inheres...'
3
Examine each definition
For each of the seven, note the reality (حقيقة) of the attribute as defined by Al-Sanusi.
4
Reflect on the mutual inseparability
The passage says the inseparability is from both sides — meaning the moral attributes and the attributes of meaning are always together.
5
Test your understanding
Use the checkpoint to confirm you can distinguish the definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسميت معنوية لأنها منسوبة للمعاني بمعنى أنها ملازمة لها. واعلم أن الملازمة من الطرفين. والصفات المعنوية هي: كونه قادراً: والكون هو الثبوت وحقيقة القادر هو المتمكن من الفعل والترك. كونه مريدا: وهي حال واجبة للذات لما قامت الإرادة بذات الله تعالى وجب وصفه بكونه مريداً. وحقيقة المريد هو الذي يرجح أحد طرفي الممكن على الطرف الآخر. كونه عالماً: هي حال واجب للذات، لما قام العلم بذات الله تعالى وجب وصفه بكونه عالماً. وحقيقة العالم هو الذي انكشف لعلمه الواجب والمستحيل والجائز. كونه حياً: هي حال واجبة للذات. لما قامت الحياة بذات الله وجب وصفه بكونه حياً. وحقيقة الحي هو الذي صح وصفه بصفات الإدراك. كونه سميعاً: هي حال واجبة للذات، لما قام السمع بذات الله وجب وصفه بكونه سميعا. وحقيقة السميع هو الذي انكشف لسمعه كل موجود. كونه بصيراً: هي حال واجبة للذات، لما قام البصر بذات الله وجب وصفه بكونه بصيرا، وحقيقة البصر هو الذي انكشف البصره كل موجود. كونه متكلماً: هي حال واجبة للذات، لما قام الكلام بذات الله وجب وصفه بكونه متكلماً، وحقيقة المتكلم الذي دل كلامه على الواجب والمستحيل والجائز.
They are called 'moral' (معنوية) because they are attributed to the meanings (معان) in the sense that they are inseparable from them. Know that the inseparability is from both sides. The moral attributes are: His being powerful: 'being' (كون) is existence, and the reality of the powerful is the one capable of action and inaction. His being willing: it is a state necessary for the Essence; when will (إرادة) inheres in the Essence of Allah, it becomes necessary to describe Him as being willing. And the reality of the willing is the one who gives preference to one of the two possibilities over the other. His being knowing: it is a state necessary for the Essence; when knowledge (علم) inheres in the Essence of Allah, it becomes necessary to describe Him as being knowing. And the reality of the knowing is the one to whom the necessary, impossible, and possible are disclosed through His knowledge. His being living: it is a state necessary for the Essence; when life (حياة) inheres in the Essence of Allah, it becomes necessary to describe Him as being living. And the reality of the living is the one for whom it is correct to be described with attributes of perception. His being hearing: it is a state necessary for the Essence; when hearing (سمع) inheres in the Essence of Allah, it becomes necessary to describe Him as being hearing. And the reality of the hearing is the one to whom every existent is disclosed through His hearing. His being seeing: it is a state necessary for the Essence; when sight (بصر) inheres in the Essence of Allah, it becomes necessary to describe Him as being seeing. And the reality of the seeing is the one to whom every existent is disclosed through His sight. His being speaking: it is a state necessary for the Essence; when speech (كلام) inheres in the Essence of Allah, it becomes necessary to describe Him as being speaking. And the reality of the speaking is the one whose speech indicates the necessary, impossible, and possible.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage defines the seven moral attributes (الصفات المعنوية) which are states (أحوال) that inhere in the Divine Essence concomitantly with the seven attributes of meaning (الصفات المعاني). The author first explains why they are called 'معنوية': they are attributed to the 'معان' (meaning-attributions) because they are inseparable from them; the inseparability is mutual (from both sides). Then he enumerates each attribute: (1) His being powerful (كونه قادراً): 'كون' means 'being' or 'existence', and the powerful one is capable of both act and refrain. (2) His being willing (كونه مريداً): a necessary state arising from the attribute of will (إرادة) inhering in the Essence; the willing one is He who gives preference to one side of possibility over the other. (3) His being knowing (كونه عالماً): a necessary state from knowledge (علم); the knowing one is He to whom the necessary, impossible, and possible are disclosed. (4) His being living (كونه حياً): a necessary state from life (حياة); the living one is He who can be described with perceptive attributes. (5) His being hearing (كونه سميعاً): a necessary state from hearing (سمع); the hearing one is He to whom every existent is disclosed via His hearing. (6) His being seeing (كونه بصيراً): a necessary state from sight (بصر); the seeing one is He to whom every existent is disclosed via His sight. (7) His being speaking (كونه متكلماً): a necessary state from speech (كلام); the speaking one is He whose speech indicates the necessary, impossible, and possible. Each definition stresses that the attribute is a 'حال واجبة للذات' (a state necessary for the Essence) that arises when the corresponding صفة معنى inheres in the Essence.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: They are distinct states (أحوال) that necessarily accompany the attributes of meaning; they are inseparable but not identical.

Key Takeaway
The seven moral attributes are necessary states that arise from the seven attributes of meaning, each defined by its specific relation to divine action, will, knowledge, life, hearing, sight, and speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that Allah's being powerful means He is truly capable of all action and inaction, in what specific area of your life are you still trying to control outcomes yourself instead of trusting His power? What step will you take this week to surrender that area to Him?
?
The attribute of being willing (مريد) means Allah gives preference to one possibility over another. Think of a disappointment or trial you are facing. How does knowing that Allah willed that specific outcome change your perspective? Will you accept His choice with contentment, or do you still harbor resentment?
?
Allah's being knowing (عالم) means He sees the necessary, impossible, and possible. What 'impossible' situation in your life—be it a broken relationship, a financial crisis, or a personal weakness—do you need to entrust to His knowledge, believing that He already knows the way through it? Write down one concrete action you will take to rely on His knowledge today.

Distinction Between Attributes of Meaning and Moral Attributes
Al-Sanusi distinguishes صفات المعاني from صفات المعنوية and notes the dispute on الحال.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between attributes of meaning (المعاني) and moral attributes (المعنوية), and how the concept of الحال (state) affects the categorization of divine attributes.

Core Idea
Attributes of meaning are real; moral attributes are mental constructs, and the disagreement about الحال changes how many categories there are.

In this lesson, Al-Sanusi clarifies a crucial distinction between two types of divine attributes: those that have external existence (المعاني) and those that are only mentally posited (المعنوية). He then notes the theological dispute over the concept of الحال (state), which directly affects how these attributes are classified. Understanding this distinction is foundational for grasping later debates in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Focus on the main distinction and the three notes.
Notice the technical terms: وجودي, ثبوتي, الحال.
2
Understand the difference between وجودي and ثبوتي
Attributes of meaning (المعاني) are existential (وجودية) - they exist both in the mind and externally. Moral attributes (المعنوية) are positive (ثبوتية) - they exist only in the mind, as mental concepts.
This is a key ontological distinction.
3
Grasp the dispute on الحال
Note that al-Qāḍī al-Bāqillānī and his followers affirm the concept of 'state' (الحال) as an intermediate entity between existence and non-existence. Al-Ashʿarī and the majority reject this, reducing moral attributes to the inherence of meanings in the Essence.
The dispute affects how many intelligibles are recognized.
4
Count the intelligibles for each view
Affirmers of الحال have four intelligibles: Essence, meanings, moral states, and names. Deniers have three: Essence, meanings, and names. Use the passage to verify.
This summarizes the impact of the dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والفرق بين المعاني والمعنوية أن المعاني وجودية تعقل ذهناً وخارجاً، والمعنوية ثبوتية تعقل ذهناً لا خارجاً). ملاحظة : الصفات المعنوية قال بها من أثبت الحال وهو القاضي الباقلاني ومن وافقه ، وقال الإمام الأشعري والجمهور بنفي الحال والمعنوية عندهم راجعة للمعاني ، وهي عبارة عن قيام المعاني بالذات. ملاحظة : إذا قلنا كونه مريداً، كونه قادراً .... إلخ، فهي صفات معنوية، وأما بدون كلمة كونه فتصبح أسماء للذات مثل مريد، قادر ...... إلخ. ملاحظة : إن من أثبت الحال عنده أربعة معقولات : الذات والمعاني والأحوال المعنوية الملازمة لها والأسماء ومن نفى الحال عنده ثلاثة معقولات : الذات والمعاني والأسماء
And the difference between the meanings (المعاني) and the moral (المعنوية) is that the meanings are existential (وجودية), conceivable mentally and externally, while the moral are positive (ثبوتية), conceivable mentally not externally. Note: The moral attributes are affirmed by those who affirm the state (الحال), which is al-Qāḍī al-Bāqillānī and those who agree with him, while al-Imām al-Ashʿarī and the majority deny the state, and the moral for them refer back to the meanings, and they are the expression of the inherence of the meanings in the Essence. Note: When we say 'His being willing', 'His being powerful', etc., these are moral attributes, but without the word 'being' (كونه) they become names for the Essence, such as 'Willing', 'Powerful', etc. Note: Whoever affirms the state has four intelligibles: the Essence (الذات), the meanings (المعاني), the moral states (الأحوال المعنوية) concomitant to them, and the names (الأسماء). And whoever denies the state has three intelligibles: the Essence, the meanings, and the names.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage first distinguishes between attributes of meaning (صفات المعاني) and moral attributes (الصفات المعنوية). Attributes of meaning are existential (وجودية), meaning they have concrete existence both in the mind and externally; moral attributes are positive (ثبوتية) and are only conceivable in the mind, not externally. Then the author provides three notes. Note 1: The moral attributes are affirmed by those who accept the concept of 'state' (الحال), namely al-Qāḍī al-Bāqillānī and his followers, while al-Imām al-Ashʿarī and the majority reject the state, and for them the moral attributes revert to the meanings, i.e., they are simply the inherence of the meanings in the Essence. Note 2: When we say 'كونه مريداً' (His being willing), this is a moral attribute; without the word 'كونه' it becomes a name of the Essence, e.g., 'مريد' (Willing). Note 3: The proponent of the state has four intelligibles: the Essence, the meanings, the concomitant moral states, and the names. The denier of the state has only three: the Essence, the meanings, and the names. Thus the affirmation or denial of الحال directly impacts how many distinct categories of attributes are recognized.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that الصفات المعنوية are real external entities like the attributes of meaning.
Correction: The passage clarifies that moral attributes are only mentally conceivable (ثبوتية تعقل ذهناً لا خارجاً), so they are not external realities.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many intelligibles does a denier of الحال have?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
The distinction between الصفات المعاني (real) and الصفات المعنوية (mental) depends on the concept of الحال, which determines whether moral attributes are considered separate categories or not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you refer to Allah as 'Willing' or 'Powerful' (omitting the word 'being'), do you ever slip into thinking these are mere labels rather than real attributes? How does the distinction between names and moral attributes sharpen your awareness of His perfections?
?
The scholars disagreed on whether moral attributes are separate from the attributes of meaning. How does your own intellectual posture toward theological nuance affect your trust in what you believe? Are you willing to engage with disagreements, or do you prefer to simplify?
?
Al-Bāqillānī saw the moral states as real mental entities. In your worship, do you feel the reality of Allah's attributes as distinct from His essence? How might affirming (or denying) the state change how you internalize His attributes in prayer and reflection?

The Debate on States and Its Impact on Divine Attribute Classification
Three notes on the two schools' views on states and their implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two major Ash'ari positions on the concept of states (ahwal) and how this disagreement affects the classification of divine attributes and intelligibles.

Core Idea
The reality of states determines whether attributes are extra entities or mental considerations.

In this passage, the commentator presents three crucial notes that clarify the difference between two major schools of Ash'ari theology: those who affirm the reality of states (ahwal) as intermediate entities between essence and attributes, and those who deny them. These notes explain how this disagreement affects the classification of divine attributes and the number of intelligible categories. Understanding these notes is key to grasping the deeper structure of Ash'ari attribute theory.

Guided Path
1
Read the first note: identify the two schools
Read the first note carefully. Note that al-Baqillani and his followers affirm states (ahwal), while al-Ash'ari and the majority deny them. For al-Ash'ari, ma'nawiyya attributes are not additional entities but merely the inherence of ma'ani in the Essence.
This is the foundational disagreement.
2
Read the second note: linguistic distinction
The second note clarifies that phrases like 'kawnuhu muridan' (His being willer) refer to ma'nawiyya attributes, but the simple word 'murid' (willer) is a name of the Essence. This shows how language reflects the underlying ontology.
The word 'kawn' signals a state.
3
Read the third note: consequences for intelligibles
The third note explains the number of intelligible categories: the affirmer of states posits four (Essence, ma'ani, states, names), while the denier posits three (Essence, ma'ani, names). This leads to a different count of attributes (e.g., twelve attributes with existence identical to Essence).
The five negative attributes are consistent across schools.
4
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider how this debate affects the understanding of divine simplicity and the relationship between essence and attributes. Why does al-Ash'ari deny states? What problem does affirming states solve?
This connects to the concept of tawhid and avoiding multiplicity in the divine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ملاحظة : الصفات المعنوية قال بها من أثبت الحال وهو القاضي الباقلاني ومن وافقه ، وقال الإمام الأشعري والجمهور بنفي الحال والمعنوية عندهم راجعة للمعاني ، وهي عبارة عن قيام المعاني بالذات (۲).
ملاحظة : إذا قلنا كونه مريداً، كونه قادراً .... إلخ، فهي صفات معنوية، وأما بدون كلمة كونه فتصبح أسماء للذات مثل مريد، قادر ...... إلخ.
ملاحظة : إن من أثبت الحال عنده أربعة معقولات : الذات والمعاني والأحوال المعنوية الملازمة لها والأسماء ومن نفى الحال عنده ثلاثة معقولات : الذات والمعاني والأسماء. وأما المعنوية عنده فراجعة للمعاني لأنها اعتبارية. ولذلك عد بعض العلماء الصفات إثني عشر فالوجود عندهم هو عين الذات وليس حالا واجبة للذات والمعنوية عبارة عن المعاني والصفات الباقية هي السلبية الخمسة)
Note: The attributive states (al-sifat al-ma'nawiyya) were upheld by whoever affirmed the state (al-haal), namely al-Qadi al-Baqillani and those who agreed with him. Al-Imam al-Ash'ari and the majority denied the state, and for them al-ma'nawiyya revert to al-ma'ani, and they are the inherence of the ma'ani in the Essence. Note: When we say 'His being willer (kawnuhu muridan), his being powerful, etc.,' these are attributive states. But without the word 'kawn' they become names of the Essence, like 'Willer, Powerful, etc.' Note: For whoever affirms the state, there are four intelligibles: the Essence, the ma'ani, the attributive states (al-ahwal al-ma'nawiyya) inseparable from them, and the names. For whoever denies the state, there are three intelligibles: the Essence, the ma'ani, and the names. And for them, al-ma'nawiyya revert to al-ma'ani because they are mental considerations. Therefore, some scholars counted the attributes as twelve, so for them existence is identical to the Essence and not a necessary state for the Essence, and al-ma'nawiyya are expressions of the ma'ani, and the remaining attributes are the five negative ones.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage presents three explanatory notes on the concept of states (ahwal) in divine attributes. The first note identifies the two major positions: al-Baqillani affirms 'al-haal' (state) as an additional entity, while al-Ash'ari and the majority deny it, considering ma'nawiyya to be nothing but the inherence of the ma'ani in the Essence. The second note clarifies the linguistic form: when we say 'kawnuhu muridan' (His being willer), that phrase refers to a ma'nawiyya attribute, but the simple word 'murid' (willer) is a name of the Essence. The third note explains the consequences for the number of intelligibles: for the affirmer of haal, there are four categories: the Essence, the ma'ani (attributes of meaning), the states inseparable from them, and the names. For the denier there are only three (Essence, ma'ani, names). Furthermore, some scholars counted twelve attributes by considering existence as identical to the Essence (not a separate state), and the remaining attributes are the five negative ones (al-sifat al-salbiyya).

Key Terms

الحال
القاضي الباقلاني
الإمام الأشعري
الجمهور
المعقولات
الصفات السلبية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the term 'ma'nawiyya' refers to the same thing for both schools, but this passage shows that for al-Ash'ari they are not additional entities but merely the inherence of ma'ani.
Correction: For al-Ash'ari, ma'nawiyya are not separate states; they are simply the existing ma'ani inhering in the Essence.

Checkpoint
According to the first note, who affirmed the states (al-ahwal)?
A) Al-Ash'ari and the majority.
B) Al-Baqillani and his followers.
C) All theologians.
D) The author of the commentary.

Key Takeaway
The affirmation or denial of states (ahwal) fundamentally changes how divine attributes are categorized, leading to different counts of intelligibles and attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe Allah as 'Willer' or 'Powerful', do you feel a distance between the name and the reality? This lesson shows that language shapes theology. How carefully do you weigh the words you use about Allah? Will you be more mindful of them starting today?
?
The scholars differed on whether states are real or mental. In your own faith, is there an area where you cling to a certain explanation simply because it's familiar, rather than seeking the truth with humility? What would it cost you to re-examine it?
?
This lesson reduces theological categories to a matter of whether states are affirmed or denied. On the Day of Judgment, you will not be asked about the number of intelligibles—but you will be asked about your worship and understanding of Allah. Does your study of these distinctions draw you closer to revering Him, or does it become a matter of intellectual pride? Be honest: where is your heart?

The Disagreement on States and the Seven Modes
Footnote detailing two views on states and the author's choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two theological positions on the reality of the seven modes and why they are counted as attributes in one school but not the other.

Core Idea
Whether the seven modes are additional states or merely the inherence of attributes divides Ashʿarī theologians.

In this footnote, the commentator explains a key dispute among the mutakallimūn: are the seven modes (being powerful, willing, etc.) extra entities called 'states' (aḥwāl) inhering in the divine Essence, or are they simply the fact that the attributes of meaning are present? Two major schools existed, and the commentator clarifies which view he follows and why.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the two positions on the states (aḥwāl).
Notice that the passage uses specific technical terms like 'al-akwān al-sabʿa' and 'amr iʿtibārī'.
2
Identify the two schools
Look for the two groups: those who affirm states (including al-Bāqillānī) and those who deny them (al-Ashʿarī and the verifiers). Understand how each defines the import of the seven modes.
The import of 'being powerful' differs: for the first school it is a state; for the second it is the inherence of power itself.
3
Analyze the implications for counting attributes
Consider why the first school counts the seven modes as attributes, while the second does not. The passage explains that if states are real, then the modes are attributes; if not, they are merely mental considerations.
4
Note the author's position
The commentator states he follows the first school in this book (Tahdhīb), but notes that al-Sanusi's shorter creed follows the second, which is considered more correct.
This shows that even within the Ashʿarī tradition there is a range of opinion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لم يتفق المتكلمون على ثبوت الحال المذكورة، بل اختلفوا فذهب جماعة منهم إلى ثبوتها وقالوا إن مدلول الأكوان السبعة أي معناها من باب الحال فمدلول كونه قادرا عندهم حال قائمة بالذات العلية زائدة على قيام القدرة بذاته تعالى وهي مدلول القادرية عندهم أيضاً، ومدلول كونه تعالى مريداً حال قائمة بذاته تعالى زائدة على قيام الإرادة بها، وهكذا باقي الأكوان وذهب الإمام الأشعري والمحققون إلى نفي الحال، وقالوا إن ثبوتها من المحال، وإن مدلول الأكوان السبعة هو قيام صفات المعاني بالذات العلية لا صفات زائدة عليه، فمدلول كونه قادراً عندهم هو نفس قيام القدرة بالذات وهو مدلول القادرية عندهم أيضاً، وهكذا باقي الأكوان وعلى المذهب الأول تعد الأكوان السبعة من الصفات لأن مدلولاتها عليه أحوال والأحوال صفات قائمة بالذات زائدة على قيام صفات المعاني بها . (غ). وقد مشينا نحن على هذا المذهب الأخير في هذا التهذيب تبعاً للإمام السنوسي في أصل الكتاب وشرحه للأنصاري، ولذا عددنا فيها تلك الأكوان من الصفات، وأما على المذهب الثاني فلا تعد الأكوان السبعة لأن مدلولها الذي هو قيام صفات المعاني بالذات ليس في الحقيقة من الصفات بل هو أمر اعتباري أي أمر ثابت في ذهن المعتبر لا في الخارج عنه ، وقد مشى الإمام السنوسي في عقيدته صغرى الصغرى على هذا المذهب وهو الأصح (س) استفدناه من شرح المارغني
The theologians did not agree on the establishment of the aforementioned state; rather they disagreed. A group of them went to its establishment and said that the import of the seven modes (al-akwan al-sab'a) – that is, their meaning – is from the category of state. So the import of 'His being powerful' for them is a state inhering in the exalted Essence, additional to the inherence of power in His Essence, and it is also the import of 'being powerful' (al-qadiriyya) for them. And the import of 'His being willing' is a state inhering in His Essence, additional to the inherence of will in it, and so on for the rest of the modes. Al-Imam al-Ash'ari and the verifiers went to the denial of the state, saying that its establishment is impossible, and that the import of the seven modes is the inherence of the attributes of meaning in the exalted Essence, not additional attributes. So the import of 'His being powerful' for them is the very inherence of power in the Essence, and that is also the import of 'being powerful' for them, and so on for the rest of the modes. According to the first school, the seven modes are counted among the attributes because their imports are states, and states are attributes inhering in the Essence additional to the inherence of the attributes of meaning in it. And we have followed this latter school in this Tahdhib, following al-Imam al-Sanusi in the original book and his commentary by al-Ansari. Therefore we have counted those modes among the attributes. But according to the second school, the seven modes are not counted as attributes because their import, which is the inherence of the attributes of meaning in the Essence, is not in reality from the attributes, but rather it is a mental consideration (amr i'tibari) – i.e., something established in the mind of the considerer, not in external reality. Al-Imam al-Sanusi in his creed 'Sughra al-Sughra' followed this second school, and it is the more correct view. We have benefited this from the commentary of al-Marghani.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This long footnote explains the two major positions on the status of the 'seven modes' (al-akwan al-sab'a) which are the attributive states such as being powerful, being willing, etc. The first group, including al-Baqillani, hold that these modes are additional states (ahwal) inhering in the divine Essence. The second group, led by al-Ash'ari, deny that they are additional entities; rather they are merely the inherence of the attributes of meaning themselves. The author of this commentary states that he follows the first school in this book (Tahdhib), counting the modes as attributes, but notes that al-Sanusi himself, in his shorter creed, followed the second school, which is the more correct view. Thus the author acknowledges that there is a distinction between the two approaches and that his counting of the seven modes as attributes follows one scholarly tradition.

Key Terms
الأكوان السبعة
الحال
صفات المعاني
أمر اعتباري
القادرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the seven modes are always considered attributes in Ashʿarī theology.
Correction: The passage shows that only one school (affirming states) counts them as attributes. The other school (led by al-Ashʿarī) denies that they are real attributes, considering them merely mental considerations.

Checkpoint
According to the footnote, what is the import of 'His being powerful' for al-Ashʿarī?
A) A state additional to the inherence of power.
B) The very inherence of power in the Essence.
C) A mental consideration only.
D) Both a state and a mental consideration.

Key Takeaway
The status of the seven modes as attributes depends on whether one affirms states as real, extra entities or denies them as mere mental considerations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The commentator acknowledges following one school in this book while noting another is 'more correct' according to al-Sanusi. Are you willing to hold a theological position that might be technically less accurate for the sake of scholarly tradition or practical ease? What does that reveal about your priorities in seeking truth?
?
If states are merely mental considerations, does that make Allah's attributes less real to you? Or does it deepen your reliance on the attributes of meaning as direct descriptions of His essence? Examine your own heart: do you find comfort in affirming extra entities or in simplifying the ontology?
?
How does the distinction between affirming and denying states affect your confidence in the reality of Allah's power and will in your daily life? If His being Powerful is just the inherence of power, does that feel less personal than if there were an additional state? The Scope of Disagreement on the Seven Modes
Sanusi clarifies the nature of the disagreement among theologians concerning the seven modes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the theological disagreement concerning the seven modes of Allah is limited to whether they are states or mental considerations, and that this does not affect their necessity.

Core Idea
The seven modes are agreed upon; the debate is about what they are, not whether they exist.

This footnote clarifies a common point of confusion. Many students of theology assume that any difference of opinion regarding Allah's attributes leads to division. However, the scholars are unanimous on the necessity of the seven modes. Let's examine exactly where the disagreement lies and what it means.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the literal meaning.
Notice that the passage starts by stating that the disagreement is 'only' about the import.
2
Identify the two positions
The passage mentions two positions: that the modes are states (ahwal) or mental considerations (i'tibariyyat). Understand the difference between these terms.
States are real but non-existent entities; mental considerations are conceptual labels.
3
Understand the scope of disagreement
Realize that the disagreement is only about the nature of the modes, not their existence or necessity for Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
علم مما قررناه قبل أن الخلاف بين المتكلمين إنما هو في مدلول الأكوان السبعة هل هو من الأحوال أو من الأمور الاعتبارية؟ ، وأما وجوب تلك الأكوان الله تبارك وتعالى فقد أجمعوا عليه كلهم وإنكاره كفر كما صرحوا به فتدبر ولا تغتر وبالله تعالى التوفيق. (غ) أي إن من أنكر كون الله عالماً وكونه قادراً .... إلخ، فإنه يكفر، أما لو قال إن كونه تعالى عالماً ليس إلا نفس اتصافه تعالى بصفة العلم، فهذا الخلاف لا يترتب عليه شيء. فالإجماع واقع على كفر من نفى المعنوية ، أما المعاني فلا.(س).
It is known from what we have established before that the disagreement among theologians is only concerning the import of the seven modes: whether it is from the category of states or from mental considerations. As for the necessity of those modes for God Almighty, all of them have consensus upon it, and denying it is disbelief, as they have stated. So reflect and do not be deceived. And with God is success. That is, whoever denies that God is knowing and that He is powerful, etc., commits disbelief. However, if someone says that His being knowing is nothing other than His very being qualified with the attribute of knowledge, then this disagreement has no consequence. The consensus pertains to disbelief for whoever denies the attributive states (al-ma'nawiyya), not the attributes of meaning (al-ma'ani).
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This footnote clarifies the theological stakes. The disagreement about whether the seven modes are states (ahwal) or mental considerations (i'tibariyyat) does not affect the fundamental necessity of affirming that God is knowing, powerful, etc. All theologians agree that these attributions are necessary. Denying them outright is disbelief. However, if one holds that the phrase 'His being knowing' is merely another way of saying He is qualified with the attribute of knowledge, that is not denial but a different interpretation. The consensus on disbelief applies to denying the actual attributive states (ma'nawiyya), not to debating the nature of those attributions.

Key Terms
الأكوان السبعة
الأحوال
الأمور الاعتبارية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Theologians disagree on whether Allah has the seven modes.
Correction: They agree on the necessity; the debate is about the nature of those modes.

Checkpoint
What is the disagreement about concerning the seven modes?
A) Whether the seven modes exist
B) Whether the seven modes are necessary for Allah
C) Whether the seven modes are states or mental considerations
D) Whether denying the seven modes is disbelief

Key Takeaway
The disagreement on the seven modes is only about their modality, not their existence or necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

This lesson shows that scholars agree on the core but differ on precision—does your own approach to faith reflect that same care?
?

Consensus and Ruling on Denying the Seven Modes
Sanusi states the consensus on the necessity of the seven modes and distinguishes between disbelief and permissible theological nuance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the consensus among theologians that the seven modes are necessary for Allah and that denying them is disbelief, while differing on their interpretation is not.

Core Idea
Denying the seven modes is disbelief, but reinterpreting them is permissible.

In the previous lesson, we saw the scope of disagreement. Now we turn to the crucial ruling: All theologians agree that the seven modes are necessary for Allah, and rejecting that necessity is clear disbelief. However, one might think that any discussion about the modes is dangerous. The footnote clarifies the boundaries.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation for this section. Pay attention to the words 'وجوب' and 'إجماع'.
2
Identify the consensus and ruling
Note that the passage states all theologians have consensus on the necessity of the seven modes, and denying them is disbelief.
The phrase 'كفر' is used strongly.
3
Distinguish between denial and reinterpretation
Understand that the gloss explains: outright denial of Allah being knowing/powerful is kufr, but saying 'His being knowing' is just His attribute of knowledge is a valid interpretation not leading to disbelief.
This distinction protects against over-extending the ruling.

Source Text

(غ) أي إن من أنكر كون الله عالماً وكونه قادراً .... إلخ، فإنه يكفر، أما لو قال إن كونه تعالى عالماً ليس إلا نفس اتصافه تعالى بصفة العلم، فهذا الخلاف لا يترتب عليه شيء. فالإجماع واقع على كفر من نفى المعنوية ، أما المعاني فلا.(س).
It is known from what we have established before that the disagreement among theologians is only concerning the import of the seven modes: whether it is from the category of states or from mental considerations. As for the necessity of those modes for God Almighty, all of them have consensus upon it, and denying it is disbelief, as they have stated. So reflect and do not be deceived. And with God is success. That is, whoever denies that God is knowing and that He is powerful, etc., commits disbelief. Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This footnote clarifies the theological stakes. The disagreement about whether the seven modes are states (ahwal) or mental considerations (i'tibariyyat) does not affect the fundamental necessity of affirming that God is knowing, powerful, etc. All theologians agree that these attributions are necessary. Definition of Ṭarū al-ʿAdam and Its Impossibility for Allah
Sanusi defines ṭarū al-ʿadam and states its impossibility for Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of ṭarū al-ʿadam (the passing of non-existence) and why it is impossible for Allah.

Core Idea
The passing of non-existence is the opposite of permanence, and only Allah is truly permanent.

This lesson introduces a key term in the study of divine attributes: ṭarū al-ʿadam (the passing of non-existence). By understanding its definition and its opposite, baqāʾ (permanence), we lay the foundation for affirming Allah's eternal existence. The passage is a concise definition-and-ruling that establishes one of the negated attributes of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the concise style: Sanusi defines the term, then immediately states the ruling.
Pay attention to the terms 'ضد' (opposite) and 'واجب' (necessary).
2
Identify the two key concepts
Identify ṭarū al-ʿadam and baqāʾ as opposites. Understand that baqāʾ is a necessary attribute of Allah, while ṭarū al-ʿadam is impossible for Him.
3
Apply the ruling
Reason: since baqāʾ is wajib (necessary) for Allah, its opposite ṭarū al-ʿadam must be mustaḥīl (impossible).
This is a direct logical implication.
4
Clarify the meaning
Note the clarification: ṭarū al-ʿadam means passing away after existence (inqiḍāʾ baʿd al-wujūd). This is not absolute non-existence but the cessation of something that once existed.
This prevents confusion with mere 'adam (non-existence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
طُرُو :العدم : وهو ضد البقاء فالبقاء واجب الله وطرو العدم مستحيل على الله تعالى والمراد بطرو العدم الانقضاء بعد الوجود.
Ṭarū al-ʿadam: it is the opposite of al-baqāʾ (permanence). Al-baqāʾ is wajib (necessary) for Allah, and ṭarū al-ʿadam is mustaḥīl (impossible) upon Allah, the Exalted. What is meant by ṭarū al-ʿadam is passing away after existence.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage defines the term ṭarū al-ʿadam (passing of non-existence) as a concept. It begins by stating that it is the opposite of baqāʾ (permanence). Since baqāʾ is a necessary attribute of Allah (wājib), its opposite – ṭarū al-ʿadam – is impossible (mustaḥīl) for Him. The passage then clarifies the meaning of ṭarū al-ʿadam: it is the cessation (inqiḍāʾ) of existence after it has been. In other words, it is the state of a thing that once existed and then becomes non-existent. For Allah, who is eternal and necessarily existent, such a state is inconceivable. This is a straightforward definition-and-ruling passage, establishing one of the divine attributes that is negated of Allah.

Key Terms
طرو العدم
العدم
البقاء
واجب
مستحيل
الانقضاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think ṭarū al-ʿadam means simply non-existence, regardless of prior existence.
Correction: Ṭarū al-ʿadam specifically means passing away after existing; it is the opposite of permanence, not absolute non-existence.

Checkpoint
What is the relationship between ṭarū al-ʿadam and baqāʾ?
A) They are synonyms.
B) Ṭarū al-ʿadam is the opposite of baqāʾ.
C) Baqāʾ is a type of ṭarū al-ʿadam.
D) They are unrelated.

Key Takeaway
Ṭarū al-ʿadam is the opposite of permanence, and since permanence is necessary for Allah, the passing of non-existence cannot apply to Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your soul knows that Allah is permanent and cannot cease. Yet you live as if your own permanence is guaranteed. When did you last contemplate your own inevitable passing away (al-inqidāʾ) and what it means that you have no control over even a single breath? What would change today if you truly lived with the certainty that you will pass away and only Allah remains?
?
You affirm that ṭarū al-ʿadam is impossible for Allah, but do you live as if your own existence is independent of His permanence? Examine your anxieties: do you fear losing things, status, or life itself more than you fear turning away from the One who never passes away? What one attachment do you need to release today because it distracts you from al-Baqī?
?
The passage defines ṭarū al-ʿadam as ‘passing away after existence.’ You know that you are existing now and will inevitably pass away. Have you prepared for that moment? Not just financially or logistically, but spiritually: have you ensured that your meeting with Allah will be one of mercy? If you were to pass away tonight, what would you regret not having done? Write it down and commit to doing it this week.

Defining al-Mumāthala and al-Ḥawādith
Sanusi defines mumāthala and ḥawādith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-mumāthala li-l-ḥawādith as the opposite of the obligatory attribute of al-mukhālafa and the meaning of al-ḥawādith as everything other than Allah.

Core Idea
Resemblance to contingents is the opposite of Allah's necessary dissimilarity.

Sanusi begins by establishing two foundational terms: 'mumāthala' (resemblance) and 'ḥawādith' (contingents). These terms are crucial for understanding why Allah cannot be compared to His creation. He then defines the world as all that is other than Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of mumāthala
Notice that mumāthala is defined as the opposite of mukhālafa, which is obligatory for Allah. Mumāthala is impossible for Allah.
Mumāthala covers resemblance in essence, attributes, and actions.
2
Understand ḥawādith
Al-ḥawādith (contingents) are things that exist after non-existence. They are everything other than Allah.
This is a key point: the world (al-ʿālam) is everything other than Allah.
3
Connect to the broader lesson
Now you know the basic definitions that will be used to prove that Allah cannot resemble any created thing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المماثلة للحوادث : هي ضد المخالفة الواجبة الله تعالى، والمماثلة مستحيلة على الله والمماثلة عبارة عن المشابهة للحوادث في الذات والصفات والأفعال. الحوادث جمع حادث، وهو الذي وجد بعد أن كان معدوماً وهو كل ما سوى الله تعالى، لأنه كان الله ولا شيء معه، ويسمى ماسوی الله سبحانه بالعالم بفتح اللام، وهو قسمان أجرام وأعراض، فالجرم ما يقوم بنفسه ولا يحتاج إلى ذات أخرى يقوم بها كذوات الحيوانات والحجر والشجر فإن كلا من هذه يقوم بنفسه، ومن صفات الجرم التحيز وهو أخذه قدر ذاته من الفراغ فكل جرم يأخذ مكاناً قدر ذاته من الفراغ الذي بين السماء والأرض، فالجرم الطويل يأخذ فراغاً مقدار ذاته في الطول والقصير يأخذ فراغاً مقدار ذاته في القصر وهكذا. والعرض ما لا يقوم بنفسه بل يحتاج إلى ذات يقوم بها كالسواد والبياض وسائر الألوان والحركة والسكون فالسواد مثلا لا يوجد بنفسه بدون ذات يقوم بها بل لا تحقق له إلا في ذات من الذوات كثوب أسود أو خشبة سوداء وهكذا. والعالم محصور في هذين القسمين والمولى سبحانه يستحيل في حقه أن يماثل شيئاً من ذلك فليس جرماً ولا عرضاً . ويستحيل على الله تعالى أن تأخذ ذاته قدراً من الفراغ، فيستحيل عليه تعالى أن يكون جرماً ، أو يكون عرضاً يقوم بالجرم. ويستحيل على الله تعالى أن يكون في جهة للجرم بأن يكون فوق العرش مثلاً أو تحته أو يمينه أو شماله أو أمامه أو خلفه، لأن الله تعالى لو كان في جهة للجرم لكان جرماً ولو كان جرماً لانتفت المخالفة ونفي المخالفة عن الله محال، وهذا من أوجه المماثلة المستحيلة باعتبار الذات وهو ما ينتجها بواسطة الجرمية. ويستحيل على الله تعالى أن تكون له جهة في نفسه بأن يكون له يمين أو شمال أو فوق أو تحت أو قدام أو خلف، لأنه لو كان كذلك لكان جرماً وهذا من أوجه الماثلة المستحيلة باعتبار الذات وهو مما ينتجها بواسطة الجرمية.
Al-mumāthala li-l-ḥawādith (resemblance to contingents): it is the opposite of al-mukhālafa (dissimilarity) which is obligatory for Allah, the Exalted, and al-mumāthala is impossible upon Allah. Al-mumāthala is the resemblance to contingents in essence (dhāt), attributes (ṣifāt), and actions (afʿāl). Al-ḥawādith (contingents) is the plural of ḥādith (contingent), which is that which exists after having been non-existent, and it is everything other than Allah, the Exalted, because there was Allah and nothing with Him. Everything other than Allah, glorified be He, is called al-ʿālam (the world), with fatḥa on the lām, and it is of two types: ajrām (bodies) and aʿrāḍ (accidents). The jirm (body) is that which subsists by itself and does not need another essence to subsist in, like the essences of animals, stones, and trees, for each of these subsists by itself. Among the attributes of the body is taḥayyuz (occupying space), which is its taking an amount of void equal to its essence; so every body takes a place equal to its essence from the void that is between the heavens and the earth. The long body takes a void equal to its essence in length, and the short takes a void equal to its essence in shortness, and so on. The ʿaraḍ (accident) is that which does not subsist by itself but needs an essence to subsist in, like blackness, whiteness, all colors, motion, and rest. For example, blackness does not exist by itself without an essence to subsist in; it has no reality except in some essence, like a black garment or a black piece of wood, etc. The world is confined to these two categories, and it is impossible in the sight of the Lord, glorified be He, to resemble any of that; so He is not a body nor an accident. And it is impossible for Allah, the Exalted, for His essence to take an amount of space; thus it is impossible for Him to be a body or an accident subsisting in a body. And it is impossible for Allah, the Exalted, to be in a direction of a body, such as being above the Throne, or below it, or to its right, or left, or front, or back, because if Allah were in a direction of a body, He would be a body, and if He were a body, dissimilarity (mukhālafa) would be negated, and the negation of dissimilarity from Allah is impossible. This is one of the aspects of impossible resemblance with regard to essence, which is produced via corporeality (jirmiyya). And it is impossible for Allah, the Exalted, to have a direction in Himself, such as having a right, left, above, below, front, or back, because if that were the case, He would be a body; this is also one of the aspects of impossible resemblance with regard to essence, produced via corporeality.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is a prolonged definition and explanatory refutation. It begins by defining al-mumāthala li-l-ḥawādith (resemblance to contingents) as the opposite of the obligatory attribute of al-mukhālafa (dissimilarity) for Allah. Resemblance is impossible for Allah, and it is defined as similarity in essence, attributes, and actions. The passage then defines al-ḥawādith (contingents) in detail: they are everything that came into existence after non-existence, i.e., everything other than Allah. Everything other than Allah is called the world (al-ʿālam), which is divided into two categories: bodies (ajrām) and accidents (aʿrāḍ). A body is defined as something that subsists by itself and occupies space (taḥayyuz); it has a specific volume. An accident cannot subsist by itself but requires a body to inhere in, such as colors, motion, and rest. The world is entirely composed of these two, and it is impossible for Allah to resemble any of them; He is neither a body nor an accident. Then the passage logically deduces further impossibilities: since Allah cannot be a body, it is impossible for His essence to occupy space, to be a body, to be an accident, or to be in a direction relative to a body (e.g., above the Throne), because being in a direction would entail being a body, which would negate dissimilarity (mukhālafa) and thus negate His divinity. Similarly, Allah cannot have a direction in Himself (e.g., having a right side), as that would also entail being a body. Both points are summarized as aspects of impossible resemblance produced through corporeality. The passage carefully follows the Sanusi method: define the term, classify terms, and then prove impossibility through rational demonstration.

Key Terms
المماثلة
المخالفة
الحوادث
العالم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think mumāthala only refers to resemblance in essence, not in attributes and actions.
Correction: The passage explicitly states mumāthala is resemblance in essence, attributes, and actions.

Checkpoint
What is the definition of al-ḥawādith according to the passage?
A) Things that exist eternally
B) Things that exist after non-existence
C) Things that move
D) Things that are bodies

Key Takeaway
Al-mumāthala means resemblance to contingents and is impossible for Allah; al-ḥawādith are all created things other than Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah is completely dissimilar to everything you know, how does that affect your mental image of Him? Are you ever tempted to imagine Him in a form or with human-like qualities? What would it mean to truly worship a Being who has no likeness?
?
Everything other than Allah is ḥawādith—contingent and brought into existence after non-existence. How does this reality strike your heart about your own existence? Do you live with a constant awareness that you are entirely dependent on Allah for every moment of your being?
?
If mumāthala (resemblance) is absolutely impossible for Allah, what does that demand from you when you describe Him to others or even in your own private thoughts? Can you let go of comparisons and surrender to a God who is beyond any category you can conceive?

Divine Attributes

Bodies, Accidents, and Divine Transcendence
Sanusi classifies the world into bodies and accidents and deduces Allah's transcendence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of the world into bodies and accidents, and why it is impossible for Allah to be a body, accident, or in any spatial direction.

Core Idea
Allah is neither a body nor an accident, and cannot be confined to space or direction.

Having defined the world as everything other than Allah, Sanusi now divides it into two categories: bodies (ajrām) and accidents (aʿrāḍ). He then uses this classification to demonstrate why Allah cannot resemble any created thing, logically excluding corporeality, spatial occupation, and direction from His essence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of a body (jirm)
A body is something that subsists by itself and occupies space (taḥayyuz). Examples: animals, stones, trees.
Taḥayyuz means taking an amount of void equal to its essence.
2
Understand the definition of an accident (ʿaraḍ)
An accident is something that does not subsist by itself but inheres in a body. Examples: colors, motion.
Accidents have no independent existence.
3
See that Allah is neither body nor accident
The world is limited to these two categories. Since Allah is not part of the world, He is neither body nor accident.
4
Appreciate the logical consequences
If Allah were a body, He would occupy space; if an accident, He would need a body. Also, being in a direction (e.g., above the Throne) would entail being a body. All these are impossible.
This preserves Allah's dissimilarity from all creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Al-mumāthala li-l-ḥawādith (resemblance to contingents): it is the opposite of al-mukhālafa (dissimilarity) which is obligatory for Allah, the Exalted, and al-mumāthala is impossible upon Allah. Al-mumāthala is the resemblance to contingents in essence (dhāt), attributes (ṣifāt), and actions (afʿāl). ?

Refutation of the Anthropomorphist Position
Sanusi refutes the Mujassimah and exposes their verbal evasion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the position of the Mujassimah regarding Allah's hands and why Ahl al-Sunnah considers it a false anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
Affirming the meaning of limbs while denying the words is still anthropomorphism.

In this passage, the author refutes the anthropomorphist position that affirms Allah has hands like creatures in meaning but avoids explicit terms like 'limb'. He exposes the contradiction and shares a telling anecdote about a leading shaykh who admits the same regarding size (qadr) and volume (hajm).

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. Notice the author's sharp criticism and personal anecdote.
2
Identify the Mujassimah's claims about the hands
They affirm two hands for Allah, meaning the same as in creatures, but avoid saying 'limb' or 'part'. Why do they avoid these terms?
They claim the terms are not found in revelation and to avoid implying separation.
3
Understand the author’s refutation
The author declares this false: Ahl al-Sunnah does not permit affirming such meanings. The hands being 'organs' in meaning is still anthropomorphism.
4
Reflect on the anecdote about qadr, hajm, and hayyiz
The author debated a shaykh who admitted that Allah has a 'size' (qadr) which is identical to 'volume' (hajm) and 'space' (hayyiz), but he avoids calling it 'volume' because it's 'not nice.' This shows the issue is only about words, not meanings.
Ponder the contradiction: avoiding a term while embracing its meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمجسمة من الحنابلة ومن تبعهم من أتباع ابن تيمية يثبتون يَدَيْنِ الله تعالى، ويقولون إن معناهما في حق الله كمعناهما في حق المخلوق إلا إنهم لا يصرحون بلفظ الجارحة والجزء لأنه لم يرد ، ولئلا يتوهم الواحد أنهما يجوز انفصالهما عن ذات الله تعالى عن هرائهم، وأن هاتين اليدين كل منهما في جهة من الأخرى، وما ذلك إلا لأنهما يعتقدون أن هاتين اليدين أعضاء وجوارح، في المعنى ولكنهم يمنعون من إطلاق اسم الجزء والجارحة عليهما. وهذا كله باطل عند أهل السنة لا يجوز إثباته الله تعالى. وقد ناقشت واحداً منهم قد تربع على عرش المشيخة صرّح بكل وضوح أن الله تعالى له قدر وأن معنى القدر عنده هو نفس معنى الحجم والحيز ، ولكنه لا يصف الله تعالى بلفظ الحجم لأن هذا اللفظ غير جميل. فالمسألة عند هؤلاء مسألة ألفاظ لا معاني فتأمل واستغرب ما شئت. (س).
And the Mujassimah (anthropomorphists) among the Hanbalis and those who follow them from the followers of Ibn Taymiyyah affirm two hands for Allah, the Exalted, and they say that their meaning with respect to Allah is like their meaning with respect to the created being, except that they do not explicitly use the words 'limb' (al-jarihah) and 'part' (al-juz') because they were not transmitted, and lest one imagine that it is permissible for them to be separated from the essence of Allah, the Exalted—by their nonsense—and that these two hands are each in a direction relative to the other. And that is only because they believe that these two hands are organs (a'da') and limbs (jawarih) in meaning, but they prevent the application of the term 'part' and 'limb' to them. All of this is false according to Ahl al-Sunnah; it is not permissible to affirm it for Allah, the Exalted. And I debated one of them who had ascended the chair of the shaykhdom; he stated with all clarity that Allah, the Exalted, has a size (qadr) and that the meaning of qadr according to him is the same as the meaning of volume (hajm) and space (hayyiz), but he does not describe Allah with the word 'volume' (hajm) because this word is 'not nice.' So the issue with these people is an issue of words, not meanings. So ponder and be amazed as you wish. (S).
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mujassimah (anthropomorphists), specifically those among the Hanbalis and followers of Ibn Taymiyyah. The author criticizes them for affirming that Allah has two hands, claiming that their meaning with respect to Allah is the same as with respect to creatures, but they avoid using explicit terms like 'limb' or 'part' because these terms are not found in revelation and to avoid the implication that the hands could be separated from Allah's essence. However, the author points out that they still believe these hands are organs and limbs in meaning. This is declared false by Ahl al-Sunnah. The author then shares a personal anecdote: he debated a prominent shaykh among them who explicitly stated that Allah has a 'size' (qadr) which is identical to 'volume' (hajm) and 'space' (hayyiz), but he avoided the word 'volume' only because it is 'not nice.' The author concludes that for these people, it is merely a matter of words, not actual meanings, and invites the reader to reflect and be amazed at this contradiction.

اليدين
الجزء
الجارحة

الحجم
الحيز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One can avoid anthropomorphism by simply not using the words 'limb' or 'part' while still affirming the same meaning as creatures.
Correction: This passage shows that Ahl al-Sunnah rejects even the meaning of limbs and organs, not just the words. The Mujassimah's avoidance of terms does not make their position acceptable.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the author refutes the Mujassimah?
A) They deny that Allah has hands entirely
B) They affirm the meaning of limbs while avoiding the terms
C) They use the word 'hand' but not 'limb'
D) They claim that Allah's hands are not real

Key Takeaway
The Mujassimah's position is false because they affirm anthropomorphic meanings while only avoiding the words; true belief must avoid both.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe Allah, do you ever feel comfortable using correct words while allowing your heart to entertain meanings that contradict His transcendence? What is one attribute where you need to purify your understanding?
?
The shaykh admitted the meaning of 'volume' but avoided the word because it's 'not nice.' In what area of your deen do you know the truth but avoid calling it what it is because it is uncomfortable? What is the cost of that avoidance?
?
Sanusi says 'the issue is an issue of words, not meanings.' Examine your own beliefs: Are you more concerned with sounding orthodox with your words, or with actually having correct meanings in your heart? What would it take to align both?

Permissibility of Using 'Dhat' for Allah
Author proves permissibility of 'dhat' via prophetic approval of a poem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that using the term 'dhat' (essence) for Allah is permissible, supported by prophetic approval of a companion's poem.

Core Idea
Prophetic approval of a poem establishes 'dhat' as a legitimate term for Allah.

Some may question whether it is permissible to apply the term 'dhat' (essence/self) to Allah. This lesson presents clear evidence from the Prophet's own approval of a poem that uses this term, showing that it is not only allowed but rooted in early Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Read the note and translation
Read the Arabic note and its English translation carefully. Notice the author's claim and the evidence provided.
The poem is attributed to the companion Habib ibn 'Adi.
2
Identify the evidence
Identify that the evidence is a poem heard and approved by the Prophet. The key line is 'fi dhati ilah' (in the essence of Allah).
3
Understand the significance
Reflect on why prophetic approval is a strong proof: it shows the term was not objectionable and was used by the Companions.
This counters any claim that 'dhat' is a later philosophical term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ملاحظة : إطلاق الذات على الله تعالى جائز كما ورد به النقل في شعر حبيب حيث: ولست أبالي حين أقتل مسلماً على أي جنب كان في الله مصرعي وذلك في ذات الإله وإن يشأ يبارك على أوصال شلو ممزع وقد سمع النبي الله هذا الشعر وأقره، والدليل في قوله وذلك في ذات الإله. ملاحظة : حقيقة العرض هو الذي لا يقوم بنفسه بل بالجرم.
Note: Applying the term 'essence' (al-dhat) to Allah, the Exalted, is permissible as transmitted in the poem of Habib where: 'I do not care, when I am killed as a Muslim, on which side I die for God; that is in the essence of Allah, and if He wills, He blesses the limbs of a torn body.' And the Prophet, peace be upon him, heard this poem and approved it. The evidence is in his saying 'that is in the essence of Allah.' Note: The reality of an accident (al-'arad) is that it does not subsist by itself but by a body (al-jirm).
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage consists of two notes. The first note establishes the permissibility of using the term 'essence' (dhat) for Allah by citing a poetic verse from the companion Habib (ra). The verse states 'that is in the essence of Allah,' and the Prophet approved the poem. Therefore, using 'dhat' for Allah is legitimate. The second note defines an accident ('arad) as that which does not exist independently but subsists through a body (jirm). This definition is foundational for understanding the nature of created attributes and contrasts with the essence of Allah.

Correction: The term is proven by a Prophetic approval of a poem using 'dhat' for Allah, showing its legitimacy.

Key Takeaway

Definition of 'Arad (Accident)
Author defines 'arad as that which subsists by a body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of an 'arad (accident) in kalam: an entity that does not subsist independently but requires a body (jirm).

Core Idea
An accident is not self-subsisting; it inheres in a body.

In Islamic theology, it is crucial to distinguish between self-subsisting entities and those that depend on others. This lesson defines the term 'arad (accident) as that which does not subsist by itself but exists through a body (jirm). This foundational concept appears throughout kalam discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic note and its translation. Note the concise definition: 'arad is that which does not subsist by itself but by a body.
The term 'arad is often translated as 'accident' in the philosophical sense.
2
Contrast with substance
Consider the opposite: a substance (jirm) subsists by itself. Understanding this contrast clarifies the definition.
A body (jirm) is a substance that can bear accidents.
3
Apply examples
Think of examples: color, motion, rest are accidents that inhere in a body. They cannot exist alone.
This definition is essential for later discussions about divine attributes.

Are you relying on your own deeds, your own strength, or your own understanding as if they were self-subsisting? What would it mean for you to let go and let Allah be the One who holds you?

The Impossibility of Time and Place for Allah
States impossibility of time and place for Allah and explains the reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why time and place are impossible for Allah and the reasoning that they are originated.

Core Idea
Time and place are created; only the created are bound by them.

This lesson establishes a foundational principle of Islamic theology: Allah is transcendent beyond time and place. The author argues that if time or place applied to Allah, He would resemble contingent beings. Read on to see the argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how the author states the impossibility first, then gives the reasoning.
Pay attention to the footnote—it contains important definitions.
2
Identify the claim and argument
The passage claims time cannot pass over Allah. What is the argument? Note the conditional: 'if time were to pass over Him, He would resemble originated things.'
3
Understand the definitions of time
The footnote gives several definitions of time, including 'the movement of the sphere' and 'the association of an imagined renewing thing with a known renewing thing.' Reflect on why these definitions matter.
The author sides with the view that time is from the stations of intellects.
4
Reflect on the underlying reason
The passage explains that time and place are originated. Only the originated are restricted by them. Since Allah is eternal, He is not restricted.
This leads to the conclusion: we do not say Allah is 'in' time or place.
5
Apply to theological discourse
Learn the permissible expressions: Allah exists before time/place, with them, and after them. This guards against anthropomorphic misconceptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويستحيل على الله الزمان، فيستحيل عليه أن يمر عليه زمان لأن الله لو مرَّ عليه زمان لماثل الحوادث (١) أي يستحيل أن تدور عليه الأفلاك أو يكر عليه الجديدان الليل والنهار والمشهور أن الزمان هو حركة الفلك ، وقيل هو مقارنة متجدد موهوم لمتجدد معلوم إزالة للإبهام، كما في قولك آتيك طلوع الشمس ، وقيل غير ذلك. واختار بعض المحققين أنه من مواقف العقول وهو الحق. (ج) وقوله إنه الحق قد لا يسلّمه له البعض. فتأمل. (س). وإنما استحال عليه تعالى التقيد بالزمان والمكان لأنهما حادثان، فلا يتقيد بهما إلا الحادث مثلهما والله سبحانه قديم فوجوده قبل الزمان والمكان ، فلا يتقيد بواحد منهما ، ولهذا لا يقال الله في مكان أو زمان لئلا يوهم تقيده بهما. نعم يجوز أن يقال الله موجود قبل المكان والزمان ومعهما وبعدهما.(غ). ويستحيل أن تتصف ذاته العلية بالصفات الحادثة مثل الحركة والسكون وغيرهما، لأن حدوث صفة يلزم منه حدوث الموصوف وهذا من أوجه المماثلة المستحيلة باعتبار الصفات وهو مما ينتجها مباشرة. ملاحظة : الفرق بين الصفة والعرض أن العرض أخص والصفة أعم، فنقول كل عرض صفة وليس كل صفة عرضاً ، فبعض الصفات عرض كصفات الحوادث وبعض الصفات ليست بعرض كصفات الله.
And time is impossible for Allah, so it is impossible for time to pass over Him, because if time were to pass over Him, He would resemble originated things (al-hawadith)—[footnote: i.e., it is impossible for the spheres to revolve around Him or for the two new things, night and day, to come back to Him. It is well known that time is the movement of the sphere, and it is said that it is the association of an imagined renewing thing with a known renewing thing to remove ambiguity, as in your saying 'I will come to you at sunrise,' and other [definitions]. Some verifiers have chosen that it is from the station of intellects, and that is the truth. (J) And his saying that it is the truth may not be accepted by some. So reflect. (S). And the reason why restriction by time and place is impossible for Him, exalted, is that they are originated, so only that which is originated like them is restricted by them. And Allah, glorified, is eternal, so His existence is before time and place, so He is not restricted by either of them. Therefore, one does not say that Allah is in a place or time, lest it suggest that He is restricted by them. Yes, it is permissible to say that Allah exists before place and time, and with them, and after them. (Gh).] And it is impossible for His exalted essence to be described by originated attributes (al-sifat al-haditha) such as motion, rest, and others, because the occurrence of an attribute necessitates the occurrence of the described, and this is one of the aspects of similarity that is impossible with respect to attributes, and it is that which directly entails it. Note: The difference between al-sifa (attribute) and al-'arad (accident) is that al-'arad is more specific and al-sifa is more general. So we say: every accident is an attribute, but not every attribute is an accident. So some attributes are accidents, like the attributes of originated things, and some attributes are not accidents, like the attributes of Allah.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is a series of doctrinal statements about what is impossible for Allah. It begins with the impossibility of time (al-zaman) passing over Allah. The argument: if time passed over Him, He would resemble originated things. A lengthy footnote elaborates: the spheres cannot revolve around Him, and night and day cannot recur for Him. It defines time as the movement of the sphere, though other definitions are given. It then explains the underlying reason: restriction by time and place is impossible because both are originated, and only the originated are restricted by them; Allah is eternal and existed before them. Hence we do not say 'Allah is in a place or time' lest it imply restriction, though we may say He exists before, with, and after them. The passage then states the impossibility of originated attributes like motion and rest for Allah. The reasoning: if an attribute occurs (comes into existence), the subject described by it must also occur (i.e., be originated), because the occurrence of an attribute necessitates the occurrence of the described. This would make Allah similar to originated things, which is impossible. Finally, a note distinguishes between sifa (attribute) and 'arad (accident): every accident is an attribute, but not every attribute is an accident. Some attributes (like those of originated things) are accidents, while others (like Allah's attributes) are not accidents.

Key Terms
zaman
makan
falk
jadidan

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think it is permissible to say Allah is 'in' a place or time.
Correction: The passage clarifies that this suggests restriction, which is impossible for Allah. Instead, we say He exists before, with, and after them.

Checkpoint
Why is it impossible for time to pass over Allah according to the passage?
A) Because time does not exist.
B) Because it would make Him similar to originated things.
C) Because He transcends all attributes.
D) Because time is eternal and He is originated.

Key Takeaway
Allah is beyond time and place; He exists before, with, and after them without being restricted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine Allah hearing your du'a, do you unintentionally place Him in a direction or location? How does knowing He is beyond space change the way you should feel His closeness?
?
You constantly live within time — fretting over past mistakes and future uncertainties. If Allah is beyond time, how must He view your worries? What does that demand from you when anxiety strikes?
?
Is there a part of your life where you act as if Allah is restricted — expecting Him to act within your timeline or in a specific place? What specific practice will you adopt to remind yourself of His transcendence?
