The Impossibility of Originated Attributes and the Distinction Between Sifa and 'Arad
States impossibility of originated attributes for Allah and defines the relationship between sifa and 'arad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Allah cannot have originated attributes like motion and rest, and how sifa differs from 'arad.

Core Idea
An attribute that begins in time would make its bearer temporal.

This lesson continues the theme of divine transcendence by addressing originated attributes. It then provides a vital clarification: not all attributes are accidents, but every accident is an attribute. Understanding this distinction is key to proper theological discourse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: first the impossibility statement, then the reasoning, then a terminological note.
2
Identify the argument against originated attributes
The author claims that if an attribute occurs (is originated), then the being described by it must also be originated. Why? Because the occurrence of an attribute entails the occurrence of its subject.
This is a logical argument: if the attribute is temporal, the subject cannot be eternal.
3
Understand the implication
If Allah had an originated attribute, He would be similar to originated things, which is impossible. So Allah's attributes are not originated; they are eternal.
4
Grasp the distinction between sifa and 'arad
The note clarifies: 'arad (accident) is a subset of sifa (attribute). Every accident is an attribute, but not every attribute is an accident. Some attributes (like Allah's) are not accidents.
Accidents are temporal attributes that inhere in a substance. Allah's attributes are not like that.
5
Apply the distinction to avoid confusion
When discussing divine attributes, we must be careful not to equate them with human accidents. This distinction safeguards tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويستحيل على الله الزمان، فيستحيل عليه أن يمر عليه زمان لأن الله لو مرَّ عليه زمان لماثل الحوادث (١) أي يستحيل أن تدور عليه الأفلاك أو يكر عليه الجديدان الليل والنهار والمشهور أن الزمان هو حركة الفلك ، وقيل هو مقارنة متجدد موهوم لمتجدد معلوم إزالة للإبهام، كما في قولك آتيك طلوع الشمس ، وقيل غير ذلك. واختار بعض المحققين أنه من مواقف العقول وهو الحق. (ج) وقوله إنه الحق قد لا يسلّمه له البعض. فتأمل. (س). وإنما استحال عليه تعالى التقيد بالزمان والمكان لأنهما حادثان، فلا يتقيد بهما إلا الحادث مثلهما والله سبحانه قديم فوجوده قبل الزمان والمكان ، فلا يتقيد بواحد منهما ، ولهذا لا يقال الله في مكان أو زمان لئلا يوهم تقيده بهما. نعم يجوز أن يقال الله موجود قبل المكان والزمان ومعهما وبعدهما.(غ). ويستحيل أن تتصف ذاته العلية بالصفات الحادثة مثل الحركة والسكون وغيرهما، لأن حدوث صفة يلزم منه حدوث الموصوف وهذا من أوجه المماثلة المستحيلة باعتبار الصفات وهو مما ينتجها مباشرة. ملاحظة : الفرق بين الصفة والعرض أن العرض أخص والصفة أعم، فنقول كل عرض صفة وليس كل صفة عرضاً ، فبعض الصفات عرض كصفات الحوادث وبعض الصفات ليست بعرض كصفات الله.
And time is impossible for Allah, so it is impossible for time to pass over Him, because if time were to pass over Him, He would resemble originated things (al-hawadith)—[footnote: i.e., it is impossible for the spheres to revolve around Him or for the two new things, night and day, to come back to Him. It is well known that time is the movement of the sphere, and it is said that it is the association of an imagined renewing thing with a known renewing thing to remove ambiguity, as in your saying 'I will come to you at sunrise,' and other [definitions]. Some verifiers have chosen that it is from the station of intellects, and that is the truth. (J) And his saying that it is the truth may not be accepted by some. So reflect. (S). And the reason why restriction by time and place is impossible for Him, exalted, is that they are originated, so only that which is originated like them is restricted by them. And Allah, glorified, is eternal, so His existence is before time and place, so He is not restricted by either of them. Therefore, one does not say that Allah is in a place or time, lest it suggest that He is restricted by them. Yes, it is permissible to say that Allah exists before place and time, and with them, and after them. (Gh).] And it is impossible for His exalted essence to be described by originated attributes (al-sifat al-haditha) such as motion, rest, and others, because the occurrence of an attribute necessitates the occurrence of the described, and this is one of the aspects of similarity that is impossible with respect to attributes, and it is that which directly entails it. Note: The difference between al-sifa (attribute) and al-'arad (accident) is that al-'arad is more specific and al-sifa is more general. So we say: every accident is an attribute, but not every attribute is an accident. So some attributes are accidents, like the attributes of originated things, and some attributes are not accidents, like the attributes of Allah.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is a series of doctrinal statements about what is impossible for Allah. It begins with the impossibility of time (al-zaman) passing over Allah. The argument: if time passed over Him, He would resemble originated things. A lengthy footnote elaborates: the spheres cannot revolve around Him, and night and day cannot recur for Him. It defines time as the movement of the sphere, though other definitions are given. It then explains the underlying reason: restriction by time and place is impossible because both are originated, and only the originated are restricted by them; Allah is eternal and existed before them. Hence we do not say 'Allah is in a place or time' lest it imply restriction, though we may say He exists before, with, and after them. The passage then states the impossibility of originated attributes like motion and rest for Allah. The reasoning: if an attribute occurs (comes into existence), the subject described by it must also occur (i.e., be originated), because the occurrence of an attribute necessitates the occurrence of the described. This would make Allah similar to originated things, which is impossible. Finally, a note distinguishes between sifa (attribute) and 'arad (accident): every accident is an attribute, but not every attribute is an accident. Some attributes (like those of originated things) are accidents, while others (like Allah's attributes) are not accidents.

Key Terms
sifa
'arad
sifat haditha

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'sifa' and 'arad' are synonymous and that Allah's attributes are accidents.
Correction: The passage clarifies that 'arad' is a subset of 'sifa'. Allah's attributes are not accidents; they are eternal and essential.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it impossible for Allah to have originated attributes?
A) Because originated attributes do not exist.
B) Because it would necessitate that Allah is originated.
C) Because Allah does not have attributes.
D) Because originated attributes are only for accidents.

Key Takeaway
Allah's attributes are not originated; they are eternal. Accidents are a type of attribute, but divine attributes are not accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah's attributes are eternal and unchanging, what does that mean for your trust in Him today? Is there an area of your life where you doubt His consistency? Will you surrender that to Him right now?
?
The passage says originated attributes would make Allah temporal—like His creation. Have you ever imagined Allah as changing emotions like a human? How does this truth shatter that false image and demand a more reverent worship from you?
?
Your own attributes (like moods, knowledge, power) are accidents—they come and go. How does the contrast between your temporary nature and Allah's eternal attributes drive you to humility and dependence on Him? What will you do today to live in light of that dependence?

The Impossibility of Size for Allah
Negates size for Allah and distinguishes true greatness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Allah cannot be described with smallness or largeness as size, and how greatness of status is affirmed for Him.

Core Idea
Allah is beyond parts; size applies only to created things.

This passage begins by establishing that Allah is not subject to size, because size implies parts. It then clarifies that the attribute 'al-Kabir' in the Quran refers to His greatness of status, not physical magnitude. Let us examine the reasoning and the Quranic proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the translation. Notice the logical structure: first negation, then distinction.
The passage uses 'sughr' and 'kibr' as size terms.
2
Understand the argument against size
The author argues that smallness and largeness are properties of parts. Since Allah has no parts, He cannot have size. This is an aspect of impossible similarity with created things.
This refutes anthropomorphism (tajsim).
3
Distinguish between two meanings of kibr
The passage clarifies that 'kibr' in the Quran means greatness of status (azamat al-sha'n), not physical largeness. It cites Quran 40:12 to support this.
Anthropomorphists wrongly interpret it as size.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويستحيل أن تتصف ذاته العلية بالصغر أو الكبر فالصغر عبارة عن قلة الأجزاء والكبر عبارة عن كثرتها، لأن الصغر والكبر من خواص الأجزاء والله تعالى منزه عن هذا. وهو من أوجه المماثلة المستحيلة باعتبار الذات وهو مما ينتجها بواسطة الجرمية. وأما الكبر بمعنى عظمة الشأن فهو واجب الله قال تعالى : ( فَالْحُكْمُ لِلَّهِ الْعَلِي الْكَبِيرِ ) (غافر : ١٢) ، فهو كبر بمعنى عظم القدرة والسلطان لا بمعنى كبر الحجم كما يقوله المجسمة. ويستحيل على الله تعالى أن يتصف بالأعراض في الأفعال أو الأحكام (٢) والأعراض (٢) ١٢: جمع غرض وهو الباعث لمراعاة المصالح ودفع المفاسد لأن ذلك بالنسبة إلى ذاته محال.

الهامش (٢): الغرض هو الأمر الباعث أي الحامل على فعل أو حكم، ويسمى سببا باعثاً وعلة باعثة، مثلاً إذا قصدت إخراج الماء من الأرض فحفرتها حتى خرج الماء فالحفر فعل وخروج الماء غرض أي أمر =
And it is impossible for His exalted essence to be described by smallness (al-sughr) or largeness (al-kibr), for smallness is an expression of few parts and largeness is an expression of many parts, because smallness and largeness are properties of parts, and Allah, exalted, is transcendent above this. And this is one of the aspects of impossible similarity with respect to essence, and it is that which entails it via corporeality. As for al-kibr in the sense of greatness of status (azamat al-sha'n), it is obligatory for Allah; He said, exalted: 'So judgment belongs to Allah, the Exalted, the Great' (Ghafir 12). It is greatness in the sense of might of power and sovereignty, not in the sense of largeness of volume as the anthropomorphists claim. And it is impossible for Allah, exalted, to be described by aghrad (purposes) in actions or judgments—(2) and aghrad (purposes) plural of gharad, which is the incentive to observe benefits and repel harms, because that, with respect to His essence, is impossible.

Footnote (2): Al-gharad is the motivating matter, i.e., the carrier (holder) for an action or judgment, and it is called a motivating cause and a final cause. For example, if you intend to extract water from the ground and you dig it until water comes out, the digging is an action and the appearance of water is a gharad, i.e., a matter =
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage contains two separate impossibilities for Allah. First, the impossibility of being described with smallness (sughr) or largeness (kibr) in the sense of few or many parts, because these are properties of parts, and Allah is transcendent above parts. This would make Him similar to originated things via corporeality (jirmiyya). The passage then distinguishes between kibr as size (which is impossible) and kibr as greatness of status (azamat al-sha'n), which is obligatory for Allah, citing Quran 40:12, and notes that this meaning refers to might of power and sovereignty, not volume as the anthropomorphists claim. Second, the impossibility of Allah being described with aghrad (purposes) in His actions or judgments. The passage defines gharad (singular) as an incentive to seek benefits and repel harms, and states that such a purpose is impossible for Allah's essence, because it would imply need. A footnote further defines gharad as the motivating cause or final cause, providing an example: digging to extract water — the digging is the action, and the appearance of water is the purpose. The footnote is incomplete in the OCR.

Key Terms
sughr (smallness)
kibr (largeness)
ajza' (parts)
jirmiyya (corporeality)
azamat al-sha'n (greatness of status)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Allah being 'al-Kabir' means He has a large physical body.
Correction: The passage clarifies that it means greatness of power and status, not volume, and that any concept of size is impossible for Allah.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it impossible for Allah to be described with smallness or largeness?
A) Because Allah is not visible.
B) Because smallness and largeness imply parts, and Allah has no parts.
C) Because the Quran does not use these terms.
D) Because humans cannot comprehend divine attributes.

Key Takeaway
Allah is free from size and parts; His attribute 'al-Kabir' signifies absolute greatness of status and power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allahu Akbar', have you ever mentally pictured a 'bigger' being? How does this lesson challenge that image, and what will you replace it with?
?
Is there an area of your life where you treat Allah's greatness as merely 'more' than creation (bigger, stronger, etc.) rather than absolutely unique and beyond comparison? What would change if you truly internalized His transcendence?
?
If you catch yourself imagining Allah in your mind with any shape or size, what concrete step will you take to train your heart to affirm His absolute freedom from such limitations?

The Impossibility of Purposes for Allah
Negates purposes (aghdhar) from Allah's actions and judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Allah cannot act for purposes (aghdhar) and what a gharad is.

Core Idea
Allah does not act for gain or to repel harm; He is self-sufficient.

This passage addresses a subtle but crucial point: Allah's actions are not motivated by any purpose that would bring benefit or repel harm, because He is free from need. The passage defines 'gharad' as the incentive behind an action. Let us explore this.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and footnote
Read the main Arabic passage and the footnote. The footnote provides an example of a human purpose to clarify the concept.
The footnote is incomplete in the OCR but still useful.
2
Understand the definition of gharad
Gharad is the incentive or motive that drives an action to achieve a benefit or repel a harm. This is what the passage negates for Allah.
In human actions, purposes are common, but for Allah they imply need.
3
Grasp why purposes are impossible for Allah
Since Allah is utterly self-sufficient and not subject to benefit or harm, He cannot have purposes. Any action of His is pure wisdom and will, not motivated by external gain.
This safeguards divine transcendence.

Impossibility of God Needing a Specifier (Mukhassis)
Author proves God cannot need a specifier and refutes Ibn Taymiyya's anthropomorphism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Allah cannot need a specifier and how this refutes anthropomorphic claims.

Core Idea
If Allah needed a specifier, He would be temporal.

This passage presents a key rational proof in Islamic theology: Allah cannot need a specifier because that would imply temporality, which is impossible for the Eternal. The author then refutes a specific claim by Ibn Taymiyya that Allah changes positions or creates attributes within Himself, labeling it as extremism in anthropomorphism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the footnote references indicating explanatory notes.
2
Identify the main proof
Find the key conditional statement: if Allah needed a specifier, it would be temporal, which is impossible.
3
Understand the definition of mukhassis
Note that mukhassis means the one who brings into existence, not merely a designator.
This clarifies the concept being refuted.
4
Reflect on the refutation of Ibn Taymiyya
See how the author rejects the idea of Allah changing positions or having temporal attributes, calling it anthropomorphism.
This guards against extreme anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويستحيل على الله تعالى أن يفتقر إلى مُخَصِّص لأنه لو احتاج إلى مخصص حادثا والحدوث على الله محال. (1) لكان (المراد من المخصص الموجد . (ج) والفاعل والمؤثر، أي إن الله تعالى لا يفتقر لا في ذاته ولا في صفاته إلى مخصص يخصصها على ما هي عليه، لأن ذلك يستلزم كون صفاته ممكنة الوجود وحادثة. ولا يقال أيضاً إن الله تعالى يخصص ذاته بالوضع المعين أو بالصفات المعينة للزوم نفس المحذور أيضاً. واعلم أن ابن تيمية أثبت ذلك المعنى في حق الله تعالى فادعى مما ادعاه أن الله تعالى يتصرف في نفسه ، بمعنى يغير أوضاع نفسه كما شاء بالحركة والنزول والصعود والجلوس على العرش وإيجاد الصفات الحادثة في ذاته مثل الغضب والرضى والحروف والأصوات وغير ذلك. وهذا غلو من ابن تيمية في التجسيم (س).
And it is impossible for Allah Most High to be in need of a specifier [mukhassis] because if He needed a specifier, it would be temporal, and temporality is impossible for Allah. (1) (The meaning of specifier is the bringer into existence, the agent, and the effective cause, i.e., Allah Most High is not in need, neither in His essence nor in His attributes, of a specifier to specify them as they are, because that would necessitate His attributes being possible of existence and temporal. Nor can it be said that Allah specifies His essence by a particular position or by particular attributes due to the same problem. And know that Ibn Taymiyya affirmed that meaning concerning Allah, claiming that Allah acts within Himself, meaning He changes the positions of Himself as He wills by movement, descending, ascending, sitting on the Throne, and creating temporal attributes in His essence such as anger, pleasure, letters, sounds, etc. And this is extremism from Ibn Taymiyya in anthropomorphism [tajsim].)
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage proves that Allah cannot need a mukhassis (specifier). The argument: needing a specifier would imply that the specifier is temporal (since specification is a temporal act), and temporality is impossible for Allah. The footnote explains that mukhassis here means the one who brings into existence. It then refutes the idea that Allah could specify His own essence or attributes, as that would also imply temporality. The footnote specifically criticizes Ibn Taymiyya for claiming that Allah changes positions and creates temporal attributes in Himself, calling this extremism in anthropomorphism (tajsim).

Key Terms
مخصص (mukhassis)
حدوث (huduth)
تجسيم (tajsim)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Allah can specify His own essence or attributes, or change His positions.
Correction: The author shows that any specification would imply temporality, which is impossible for Allah.

Checkpoint
Why is it impossible for Allah to need a specifier?
A) Because a specifier would have to be eternal.
B) Because needing a specifier would imply that Allah is composite.
C) Because needing a specifier would imply temporality in Allah.
D) Because a specifier would be impossible to exist.

Key Takeaway
Allah cannot need a specifier because that would make Him temporal, and He is eternally self-sufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine Allah, do you ever unconsciously picture Him in a particular place or with a form? How does this lesson challenge that mental image, and what steps will you take to purify your belief?
?
The author refutes Ibn Taymiyya's view that Allah changes positions. Have you ever heard or believed that Allah literally 'descends' or 'sits'? What is at stake if we take such descriptions literally, and how will you guard your faith against this?
?
This lesson shows that even thinking of Allah as 'choosing' attributes implies need. Do you ever mentally attribute human-like processes to Allah, like deciding or changing His mind? What specific thought pattern will you correct today?

God's Oneness in Essence
Sanusi states the necessity of God being one in essence, not composite and without peer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah must be one in essence, meaning He is not composite and has no peer.

Core Idea
Allah's essence is absolutely one: no parts, no equal.

This lesson focuses on the first aspect of tawhid: oneness of essence. Before considering attributes or actions, we must affirm that Allah's very being is unique and indivisible. Sanusi begins by stating the impossibility of Allah not being one, then clarifies what that means.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage on essence
Read the Arabic sentence stating that Allah is not composite in His essence and has no peer in essence.
The phrase 'لا مركباً في ذاته' means 'not composed of parts'.
2
Understand 'no peer in essence'
Consider that having a peer would imply two beings with identical essential attributes, which would negate absolute uniqueness.
3
Reflect on necessity
Ask yourself: Why must the Necessary Being be one in essence? This connects to the impossibility of a composite being depending on its parts.
This is a foundational principle in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويستحيل أن لا يكون واحداً : فيجب أن يكون الله واحداً لا مركباً في ذاته، أو يكون له مماثل في ذاته أو في صفاته أو يكون معه في الوجود مؤثر في فعل من الأفعال(٢). فيستحيل أن يكون لأحد من المخلوقين صفات كصفات الله بأن تكون له قدرة توجد وإرادة تخصص وهكذا إلى آخر الصفات. وهذا مستحيل لأن الله لا شريك له في صفاته وهذا هو الكم المنفصل في الصفات، وهو محال، وكذا يستحيل عليه تعالى الكم المتصل في الصفات، أي يجب أن لا تكون صفاته متعددة ومن قال بالتعدد فهو محجوج بالإجماع، وقال بعضهم الكم المتصل في الصفات لا ينا في التوحيد، لكنه خطأ وخلاف الإجماع. ويستحيل على الله تعالى أن يكون معه شريك في الأفعال بأن يكون غيره يفعل كفعله، وهذا هو الكم المنفصل في الأفعال. (٢) (فيه رد على المعتزلة في قولهم بأن العبد يخلق أفعال نفسه الاختيارية بقدرة خلقها الله فيه، والصحيح عدم كفرهم بذلك لأنهم لم يجعلوا خالقية العبد كخالقية الله تعالى، وذهب علماء ما وراء النهر إلى تكفيرهم، بل جعلوا المجوس أسعد حالاً منهم ، لأنهم لم يثبتوا الله تعالى إلا شريكاً واحداً وهؤلاء أثبتوا الله شركاء كثيرة. (ج).
And it is impossible that He not be one: so it is necessary that Allah be one, not composite in His essence, nor having a peer in His essence or in His attributes, nor having with Him in existence an effecter in any action. Thus it is impossible for any of the created beings to have attributes like the attributes of Allah, such that they have a power that brings into existence and a will that specifies, and so on to the end of the attributes. This is impossible because Allah has no partner in His attributes, and this is the discrete quantity [al-kamm al-munfasil] in attributes, and it is impossible. Likewise, continuous quantity [al-kamm al-muttasil] in attributes is impossible for Him, i.e., His attributes must not be multiple, and whoever says they are multiple is refuted by consensus. Some said continuous quantity in attributes does not harm tawhid, but it is wrong and contrary to consensus. And it is impossible for Allah to have a partner in actions, i.e., that another does like His action, and this is the discrete quantity in actions. (2) (It contains a refutation of the Mu'tazila in their saying that the servant creates his own voluntary actions by a power Allah created in him. The correct view is that they are not disbelievers because they did not make the servant's creativity like Allah's creativity. The scholars beyond the river went to declaring them disbelievers, even considering the Magi more fortunate than them, because they only affirmed one partner for Allah, while these affirmed many partners.)
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage establishes the necessity of God's oneness in three respects: essence (not composite), attributes (no peer), and actions (no partner in any effect). It then explains that having a partner in attributes is termed 'discrete quantity' (al-kamm al-munfasil) and is impossible. 'Continuous quantity' (al-kamm al-muttasil) in attributes (i.e., multiple attributes) is also impossible; whoever claims multiplicity is refuted by consensus, though some erroneously think it does not harm tawhid. Similarly, having a partner in actions is discrete quantity in actions. The footnote refutes the Mu'tazila who claim that the servant creates his own voluntary actions; the author states the correct view is that they are not disbelievers because they do not make the servant's creativity equal to Allah's, but some scholars declare them disbelievers.

Key Terms
واحد (wahid)
مركب (murakkab)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God having parts or a peer is possible.
Correction: Sanusi shows it is impossible because the Necessary Being cannot depend on anything, and having a peer would imply limitation.

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi mean by 'لا مركباً في ذاته'?
A) Allah is not composed of parts.
B) Allah has no attributes.
C) Allah is not a body.
D) Allah is simple in His actions.

Key Takeaway
Allah's essence is absolutely one: indivisible and without any similar being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever feel that your understanding of God is a patchwork of ideas from culture, upbringing, and personal reasoning, rather than the pure oneness this passage teaches? How can you actively purify your concept of Allah from any hidden assumptions of composition or similarity? Commit to reciting Surat al-Ikhlas with this meaning daily.
?
The passage says it is impossible for God not to be one. Pause: Do you live as if there is any other power or being that shares His exclusive authority? Identify one area of your life where you fear something other than Allah, and repent.
?
Sanusi defines oneness by negation: no parts, no equal. When you say 'Allahu Akbar', do you truly feel that nothing is like Him, or have you reduced it to a habit? This lesson demands a total reorientation of your heart's understanding. What concrete step will you take today to break the habit of empty proclamation and instead internalize His absolute uniqueness?

God's Oneness in Attributes: Discrete and Continuous Quantity
Sanusi explains the two types of impossible partnership in attributes: discrete and continuous quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of impossible partnership in attributes: discrete quantity (a creature having a like attribute) and continuous quantity (multiple attributes being divisible).

Core Idea
No creature can share Allah's attributes, nor can His attributes be multiple in a way that implies composition.

Having established oneness in essence, Sanusi now turns to attributes. He explains that both having a peer in attributes (discrete quantity) and having attributes that are multiple in a divisible sense (continuous quantity) are impossible and rejected by consensus. This lesson clarifies these technical terms and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage on attributes
Read from 'أو في صفاته' to 'خلاف الإجماع' and note the two types of quantity mentioned.
Discrete quantity means separate entities; continuous quantity means a whole with parts.
2
Identify discrete quantity in attributes
See how Sanusi describes a creature having an attribute like Allah's (e.g., power to create) as discrete quantity, which is impossible.
This is shirk because it gives a creature a divine attribute.
3
Understand continuous quantity in attributes
Sanusi says it is impossible for Allah's attributes to be multiple in a way that implies composition or divisibility, though some mistakenly thought it harmless.
The consensus refutes that view.

Source Text

God's Oneness in Actions and the Mu'tazila Error
Sanusi denies any partner in actions and uses a footnote to refute the Mu'tazila claim that humans create their own actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that no creature can truly cause an act like Allah does, and that the Mu'tazila view on human action creation is a form of shirk according to some scholars.

Core Idea
Allah alone creates all actions; claiming creatures create their own actions partners Him.

The final aspect of oneness is in actions: no being can be an effecter like Allah. Sanusi then addresses the Mu'tazila who claimed humans create their own voluntary actions by a power given by Allah. The footnote explains the spectrum of opinion on whether this constitutes disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on actions and the footnote
Read the sentence denying a partner in actions and then the footnote refuting the Mu'tazila.
The footnote is part of the source material.
2
Understand 'discrete quantity in actions'
This means another being performing an action equal to Allah's act. Sanusi declares it impossible.
3
Analyze the Mu'tazila position
The Mu'tazila say the servant creates his own actions. The author says the correct view is that they are not disbelievers, but some scholars declared them disbelievers.
This is a nuanced sectarian discussion.

Source Text

?

?

?

This lesson teaches nuance—how can you balance upholding tawheed with extending the benefit of the doubt? Before judging, resolve to learn their full position and gently clarify the truth as taught by the Salaf.

Al-Kam: How Shirk is Impossible in Attributes and Actions
Defines al-kam al-munfasil and al-kam al-muttasil in attributes and actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of impossibility of partnership with Allah regarding His attributes and actions, as defined by al-kam al-munfasil and al-kam al-muttasil.

Core Idea
Allah has no partner in His attributes or actions — this is the meaning of al-kam.

Sanusi continues his discussion of tawhid by addressing the possibility of a partner sharing in Allah's attributes or actions. He introduces two technical terms: al-kam al-munfasil and al-kam al-muttasil. These terms clarify how any suggestion of a divine partner is rationally and theologically impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the three distinct parts: attributes (separate and connected) and actions.
2
Distinguish between al-kam al-munfasil and al-muttasil
Identify the key difference: al-munfasil refers to a creature having an attribute like Allah's; al-muttasil refers to multiplicity within Allah's own attributes. Both are impossible.
Al-munfasil in attributes means a separate entity sharing a similar attribute; al-muttasil means internal multiplicity.
3
Note the refutation of a wrong view
Sanusi explicitly refutes those who claim that multiplicity in attributes does not contradict tawhid. Understand the consensus argument against them.
This is a key point: some erroneously thought multiple attributes imply composition.
4
Recognize the application to actions
The same type of impossibility applies to actions: no creature can act like Allah's act. This is al-kam al-munfasil in actions.
Actions here mean creative actions, like bringing into existence.
5
Reflect on the consensus
Sanusi appeals to ijma' (consensus) to reject both types of shirk. Internalize that this is a settled matter in orthodox theology.
Consensus is a powerful evidence in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن يكون لأحد من المخلوقين صفات كصفات الله بأن تكون له قدرة توجد وإرادة تخصص وهكذا إلى آخر الصفات. وهذا مستحيل لأن الله لا شريك له في صفاته وهذا هو الكم المنفصل في الصفات، وهو محال، وكذا يستحيل عليه تعالى الكم المتصل في الصفات، أي يجب أن لا تكون صفاته متعددة ومن قال بالتعدد فهو محجوج بالإجماع، وقال بعضهم الكم المتصل في الصفات لا ينافي التوحيد، لكنه خطأ وخلاف الإجماع. ويستحيل على الله تعالى أن يكون معه شريك في الأفعال بأن يكون غيره يفعل كفعله، وهذا هو الكم المنفصل في الأفعال.
That one of the created beings should have attributes like the attributes of God, such as a power that brings into existence and a will that specifies, and so on to the rest of the attributes. This is impossible because God has no partner in His attributes, and this is the separable quantity (al-kam al-munfasil) in attributes, and it is impossible. Likewise, the connected quantity (al-kam al-muttasil) in attributes is impossible for Him, i.e., His attributes must not be multiple, and whoever says they are multiple is refuted by consensus; and some said that the connected quantity in attributes does not contradict tawhid, but this is an error and against consensus. And it is impossible for God to have a partner in actions, that someone else should act like His action, and this is the separable quantity in actions.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage defines two types of impossibility regarding partnership with God. First, in attributes: 'al-kam al-munfasil' means a creature possessing an attribute exactly like God's attribute (e.g., power that creates). 'Al-kam al-muttasil' means multiplicity in God's own attributes—some claimed that multiple attributes imply composition, but consensus rejects that. Second, in actions: a partner doing acts like God's acts is also impossible. The author refutes those who accept multiplicity in attributes as not contradicting tawhid.

Key Terms
الكم المنفصل
الكم المتصل
شريك
صفات الله
توحيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some claim that multiplicity of attributes (al-kam al-muttasil) does not contradict tawhid.
Correction: This is an error and against consensus; attributes are multiple in the sense of distinct meanings, but not in a way that implies composition or a partner in divinity.

Checkpoint
What is 'al-kam al-munfasil' in attributes?
A) Multiplicity of attributes within God
B) A creature having an attribute like God's attribute
C) God having a partner in actions
D) The impossibility of God having attributes

Key Takeaway
Allah has no partner in His attributes or actions, and any suggestion of such is impossible by consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself attributing the power to truly change your situation to anything other than Allah—like your own effort, another person, or 'luck'? How does this lesson's teaching about al-kam al-munfasil in actions challenge that tendency?
?
When you consider the perfection of Allah's attributes, does your heart ever subtly imagine that someone else—a scholar, a saint, even yourself—could possess a sliver of that perfection independently? What would it mean to purge your heart of even that smallest trace of shirk?
?
This lesson declares that any suggestion of multiplicity in Allah's attributes is against consensus. Have you ever entertained thoughts or beliefs that implied parts within Allah? How can you train your mind to reject such notions firmly?

Dual Attribution of Actions and Etiquette Regarding Evil
Author explains dual attribution and etiquette of evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why voluntary actions are attributed to both God and human beings, and the proper manners of attributing evil.

Core Idea
Actions are attributed to God as Creator and to humans as acquirers; only good is attributed to God.

This passage introduces the key Ash'ari doctrine of kasb, explaining how one action can have two agents from different aspects. It also establishes the essential etiquette of never attributing evil directly to God, even though He creates it.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation to get the flow.
Pay attention to the analogies used.
2
Identify the dual attribution
Notice that the voluntary action is attributed to God as creation (khalq) and to the human as acquisition (kasb).
This is the core idea.
3
Examine the analogies
The author compares this to attributing limbs to a person (even though created by God) and to a rented house (owner vs. tenant).
These illustrate how one thing can have two attributions.
4
Understand the rule about evil
Even though evil is created by God, good manners require attributing good to God and evil to the self, to avoid implying approval.
This is a key etiquette point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولأجله يضاف الفعل الاختياري إلى الحي وينسب إليه كما تضاف وتنسب إليه أعضاؤه كرأسه ويده ورجله مع أنها مخلوقة الله وحده. فالفعل الاختياري ينسب إلى الله خلقاً وللحي كسبا، ويصح نسبة شيء واحد لفاعلين مختلفين بجهتين مختلفتين كالدار المستأجرة تنسب لمالكها بجهة الملك ولمستأجرها بجهة الانتفاع. ومع أن الشر مخلوق الله تعالى على فرض أنه وجودي، لكن الأدب أن لا ينسب الله تعالى إلا الحسن فينسب الخير لله والشر للنفس وإن كان منسوبا خلقا لله، وذلك لأن نسبة الشر يتبادر منها عادة اكتسابه والرضى به، ومعلوم أن الله تعالى لا يرضى بالشر. (۱) معلوم أن هذا التشبيه بين إضافة الرأس للعبد وبين إضافة الفعل الاختياري له، إنما هو من حيث مطلق الإضافة فقط، لأننا نعلم أن الرأس لا يقال فيه إن قدرة العبد متعلقة به على سبيل الكسب، ولكن أريد هنا أنه كما لا يبعد أن يضاف إلى العبد أمر اتفق على أنه غير مخلوق له كالرأس، فكذلك لا يبعد أن يضاف إليه فعله الاختياري على سبيل الكسب لا الخلق، ويضاف إلى الله تعالى على سبيل الخلق لا الكسب فتأمل. (۲) المنع من نسبة الشر إلى الله تعالى إنما هو بسبب أن البعض قد يتوهم أن نسبة الشر إليه تعالى تقتضي أن يكون شريرا، ومع أن هذا باطل، لأن الشرير هو من اكتسب الشر لا من خلقه، والكسب فيه جهة اتصاف، والخلق مجرد فعل في الحقيقة لا صفة كما بينا قبل ذلك في صفات الأفعال، وقلنا إن قولنا "صفات الأفعال" إنما هو على سبيل التجاوز لأن الفعل في الحقيقية ليس صفة، فالصفة تقتضي أن تكون قائمة بالموصوف، والفعل لا يقوم بالله، لحدوثه. فلذلك فإننا نقول بمنع نسبة الشر إلى الله تعالى دفعاً لما يتوهم اقتضاء ذلك كونه تعالى شريرا، ومع بطلان هذا إلا أن الاحتياط في مثل ذلك ألزم، والمنع على سبيل الأدب، فيصرح في مقام التعليم أن الله خالق الشر والخير، ويبين عدم اقتضاء ذلك لكونه تعالى شريرا. واعلم أن بعض العلماء حقق أن الشر أمر لا وجود له، لأنه يرجع في الحاصل إلى عدم الناس من... جار على نسق واحد مع باقي الصفات، كما ترى والمخالف يلزمه التشبيه بلا ريب فمن ذلك تعلم أن مخالفة كثير من الفرق للسادة الأشاعرة والماتريدية في كون القدرة الحادثة إنما هي على سبيل الكسب، مخالفة باطلة، وادعاؤهم أن هذا أمر غير مفهوم كلام متناقض مع ما يقررونه هم أي سائر الفرق المخالفة - في - سائر الصفات من مخالفة الله تعالى لسائر الحوادث. ويشذ عن هذا القول المجسمة من الحنابلة والمنتسبين إلى السلف الصالح وهم منهم براء، لأنهم يماثلون في الحقيقة بين الخالق والمخلوق في حقائق سائر الأمور، ويميزون بينهما في الشكل والكيفية فقط. فهؤلاء ربما لا ينفون أن تكون قدرة العبد خالقة، وربما ينسبون ذلك كعادتهم إلى الفطرة والبداهة، وهم في ذلك مخالفون لسائر الفرق والعقلاء. فتأمل في هذا فإنه يحل إشكالات عديدة، ويوضح قول أهل السنة. (س)
Because of it (kasb), the voluntary action is attributed to the living being and ascribed to him, just as his limbs like his head, hand, and foot are attributed and ascribed to him, even though they are created by God alone. So the voluntary action is attributed to God as creation and to the living being as acquisition (kasb). It is valid to attribute one thing to two different agents from two different aspects, like a rented house: it is attributed to its owner from the aspect of ownership and to its tenant from the aspect of use. And even though evil is created by God most high, on the assumption that it is existential, nevertheless good manners require that only good be attributed to God, so good is attributed to God and evil to the self, even though it is attributed to God as creation. This is because attributing evil usually implies its acquisition and approval, and it is known that God does not approve of evil. (1) It is known that this analogy between the attribution of the head to the servant and the attribution of his voluntary action to him is only from the aspect of absolute attribution. For we know that the head is not said to have the servant's power related to it on the basis of kasb; but what is intended here is that just as it is not unlikely that something agreed to be not created by the servant be attributed to him, like the head, so likewise it is not unlikely that his voluntary action be attributed to him on the basis of kasb, not creation, and be attributed to God on the basis of creation, not kasb. (2) The prohibition of attributing evil to God is because some might mistakenly think that attributing evil to Him implies that He is evil. Yet this is false, because the evil person is one who acquires evil, not one who creates it. Acquisition involves an aspect of qualification, while creation is merely an act in reality, not an attribute, as we explained before regarding acts of attributes. We said that our saying 'attributes of acts' is only a manner of speaking, because the act in reality is not an attribute; the attribute requires that it inhere in the described, while the act does not inhere in God due to its temporality. Therefore, we say to prohibit attributing evil to God to ward off what is mistakenly thought of that implying He is evil. Even though this is false, caution in such matters is more binding, and the prohibition is a matter of good manners. So in the context of teaching, it is stated that God is the Creator of evil and good, and it is explained that this does not imply that He is evil. And know that some scholars have verified that evil has no existence, because it ultimately reduces to non-existence... It follows the same pattern as the other attributes, as you see, and the opponent is inevitably led to anthropomorphism. From this you learn that the opposition of many sects to the Ash'ari and Maturidi masters regarding the created power being only on the basis of kasb is a false opposition. Their claim that this matter is incomprehensible is self-contradictory given what they themselves affirm—all other opposing sects—regarding the other attributes of God's dissimilarity to all temporal things. Excluded from this statement are the anthropomorphists among the Hanbalis and those claiming to follow the righteous predecessors, who are innocent of them, because they actually liken the Creator to the created in the realities of all matters, and distinguish between them only in form and quality. These perhaps do not deny that the servant's power is creative, and perhaps they attribute that, as usual, to natural disposition and intuition. In this they oppose all other sects and rational people. So reflect on this, for it resolves many problems and clarifies the statement of the Ahl al-Sunna.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage explains the dual attribution of voluntary actions and the proper etiquette regarding evil. The author states that because of kasb, a voluntary action is attributed to the human (the living being) in the same way that limbs like the head, hand, and foot are attributed to him, even though all are created by God. While the action is attributed to God as creation (khalq), it is attributed to the human as kasb. This dual attribution is likened to a rented house, which belongs to the owner by ownership and to the tenant by use. Then the author discusses evil. Even if evil is created by God (assuming it is something positive), proper manners dictate that only good be attributed to God. Thus good is attributed to God, and evil is attributed to the human soul, even though in terms of creation it is from God. The reason is that attributing evil to God might be misunderstood to imply that God approves of it or is evil oneself, whereas God does not approve of evil. Two footnotes clarify: one explains that the analogy with limbs is only about attribution, not about power; the other explains that the prohibition on attributing evil to God is a precautionary measure to avoid the false implication of evil in God, even though creating evil does not make one evil. The author then returns to the main argument, stating that the doctrine of kasb is consistent with the way other attributes (like knowledge, existence) are affirmed for God and creatures while acknowledging a difference in reality. He concludes that those who reject kasb inevitably fall into anthropomorphism, and he singles out the anthropomorphists among the Hanbalis who liken God to creation in essence and only differ in form. He urges reflection, stating that understanding this resolves many difficulties and clarifies the Sunni position.

Key Terms
نسبة
خلق
كسب
شر
أدب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That attributing evil to God as its Creator makes God evil.
Correction: Evil is created by God, but He does not acquire it; the evil person is the one who acquires evil. Creation alone does not imply evil character.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it considered improper (against good manners) to attribute evil directly to God?
A) Because God did not actually create evil.
B) Because someone might wrongly conclude that God is evil or approves of evil.
C) Because evil is only a privation, not a creation.
D) Because evil should be attributed to Satan alone.

Key Takeaway
Voluntary actions are attributed to God as creation and to humans as acquisition, and it is improper to attribute evil to God due to the implied approval.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a great evil or misfortune, where does your heart instinctively place the blame? Have you ever harbored resentment toward God for something that happened to you, and how does this lesson's distinction between creation and acquisition challenge that feeling? Will you commit to catching yourself the next time you are tempted to attribute evil to God, and instead reflect on your own responsibility?
?
This passage says good manners demand that evil not be attributed to God. Think of a specific time you spoke about a tragic event—did your words protect God from blame or did they indirectly suggest God is the source of evil? How can you change your speech to align with the etiquette taught here, starting today?
?
If all actions, including your sins, are created by God but acquired by you, what does that say about your personal accountability? Have you ever used the idea of destiny to excuse your own failings? How will you now embrace full responsibility for your choices while still acknowledging God's role as Creator?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
7 min
Prohibition of Attributing Evil and Refutation of Anthropomorphism
Author defends kasb and refutes anthropomorphism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari doctrine of kasb refutes anthropomorphism and how it aligns with the principle of God's dissimilarity to creation.

Core Idea
Rejecting kasb leads to anthropomorphism; God's dissimilarity to creation is maintained through correct attribution.

This passage defends the Ash'ari-Maturidi position on kasb by showing that the alternative inevitably leads to anthropomorphism. The author argues that those who deny kasb end up likening God to creation in essence, violating tanzih.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage and translation, focusing on the argument linking kasb to anthropomorphism.
The passage continues from the previous lesson.
2
Understand the precautionary prohibition
The author explains that prohibiting the attribution of evil to God is a precaution to avoid misunderstanding, even though creating evil does not make one evil.
This reinforces the etiquette from the previous lesson.
3
Link kasb to divine attributes
The author shows that just as God's attributes (like knowledge) are incomparable to human attributes, so too is creation incomparable to acquisition.
This is the key to avoiding anthropomorphism.
4
Identify the error of anthropomorphists
The author criticizes those who reject kasb and end up likening God to creation in reality (essence) rather than just in form.
This includes some Hanbalis and pseudo-Salafis.
5
Reflect on the broader significance
Understanding this resolves many problems and clarifies the Sunni position.
The author urges deep reflection.

Misconception & Correction

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence for those who reject the distinction between God's creation and human acquisition (kasb)?
A) They affirm God's transcendence more clearly.
B) They inevitably fall into anthropomorphism (tashbih).
C) They become atheists.
D) They adopt the position of the Ash'aris.

Key Takeaway
Rejecting the Ash'ari doctrine of kasb leads to anthropomorphism, as it fails to maintain God's absolute dissimilarity from creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a tragedy and your heart whispers that God is being unfair, do you stop to remind yourself that He creates but does not acquire evil? How can you train your tongue to say 'God created this' without letting resentment settle in your heart?
?
The author warns that denying kasb leads to a subtle anthropomorphism. Where in your own thoughts do you inadvertently compare God's actions to human actions? What specific step will you take this week to reinforce your belief in tanzih?
?
If someone asks you, 'How can a good God create evil?', can you articulate the distinction between creation and acquisition from this lesson? Commit to memorizing the key phrase: 'The evil person is one who acquires evil, not one who creates it.' How will you use this to defend God's perfection in your next conversation?

Divine Attributes

Why Evil Is Not Attributed to God
Explains the prohibition and kasb distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is prohibited to attribute evil to God and how the concepts of kasb and sifat al-af'al clarify that creation does not entail evilness.

Core Idea
Creating evil does not make God evil; only acquiring evil does.

This lesson explains a crucial theological precaution: although God creates both good and evil, attributing evil to Him is prohibited to avoid misunderstanding. The passage clarifies that creation is not an attribute, and the evil one is the acquirer, not the creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall argument.
Note the careful language about 'attribution' and 'acquisition'.
2
Identify the reason for prohibition
Find why scholars prohibit attributing evil to God. The passage states it is to prevent misunderstanding.
The misunderstanding is that God would be considered evil.
3
Understand the kasb distinction
Notice the distinction between the acquirer and the creator. Only the acquirer is evil.
Creation does not imply approval or evilness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۲) المنع من نسبة الشر إلى الله تعالى إنما هو بسبب أن البعض قد يتوهم أن نسبة الشر إليه تعالى تقتضي أن يكون شريرا، ومع أن هذا باطل ، لأن الشرير هو من اكتسب الشر لا من خلقه، والكسب فـيـه جهــة اتصاف، والخلق مجرد فعل في الحقيقة لا صفة كما بينا قبل ذلك في صفات الأفعال، وقلنا إن قولنا "صفات الأفعال" إنما هو على سبيل التجاوز لأن الفعل في الحقيقية ليس صفة، فالصفة تقتضي أن تكون قائمة بالموصوف ، والفعل لا يقوم بالله، لحدوثه. فلذلك فإننا نقول بمنع نسبة الشر إلى الله تعالى دفعاً لما يتوهـ اقتضاء ذلك كونه تعالى شريراً، ومع بطلان هذا إلا أن الاحتياط في مثل ذلك ألزم، والمنع على سبيل الأدب، فيصرح في مقام التعليم أن الله خالق الشر والخير، ويبين عدم اقتضاء ذلك لكونه تعالى شريراً. واعلم أن بعض العلماء حقق أن الشر أمر لا وجود له، لأنه يرجع في الحاصل إلى عدم أمر ما. وبالتالي فليس مخلوقاً أصلاً ، لأن المخلوق لا يكون عدمياً. وبالتالي يتم الخروج مطلقاً الإشكال المشهور وهو أن الله إذا كان خالقا للشر فهو شرير هذا مع أننا بينا أعلاه أن خالق الشر لا يقال عنه شرير بل مكتسب الشر هو الشرير. وفي مسألة الشر كلام دقيق مبين في المطولات.(س).
(2) The prohibition of attributing evil to God Almighty is only because some might mistakenly think that attributing evil to Him entails that He is evil. And yet this is false, because the evil one is the one who acquires evil, not the one who creates it. And acquisition (kasb) involves an aspect of qualification, while creation is merely an act in reality, not an attribute, as we explained previously regarding the attributes of actions (sifat al-af'al). And we said that our saying 'attributes of actions' is merely a manner of speaking (tajawuz) because an action in reality is not an attribute, for an attribute requires that it subsist in the described, and an action does not subsist in God due to its temporality. Therefore, we say that attributing evil to God Almighty is prohibited in order to repel what might be imagined that it entails that He is evil. And even though this is false, nevertheless caution in such matters is more imperative, and the prohibition is a matter of proper conduct (adab). So in a teaching context, one states that God is the creator of evil and good, and one explains that this does not entail that He is evil. And know that some scholars have verified that evil is a non-existent thing (amr la wujud lahu), because it reduces in the end to the non-existence of something. Consequently, it is not created at all, because the created cannot be non-existent. And thereby one entirely escapes the famous problem: that if God is the creator of evil then He is evil. This is in addition to what we stated above that the creator of evil is not called evil; rather, the acquirer of evil is the evil one. And concerning the issue of evil there is precise discussion explained in the longer works. (S)
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage is a marginal commentary explaining why evil should not be attributed to God and discussing the nature of evil. It begins by stating the reason for prohibition: to prevent the misunderstanding that attributing evil to God implies He is evil. It clarifies that the evil one is the acquirer (muktasib) of evil, not its creator. Creation is not an attribute; it is a transient act, and God's creation of evil does not describe His essence. Then it points out that some scholars hold that evil is essentially non-existent (amr 'adami), so it is not created at all. This solves the problem of God creating evil: since evil is non-existent, it is not created. The passage also reiterates that even if evil is created, the creator is not evil—only the acquirer. The discussion is precise and found in longer works.

Key Terms
شر
كسب
صفات الأفعال
خلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that if God creates evil, then God must be evil.
Correction: The passage explains that creation is not an attribute, and only the acquirer of evil is called evil.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it prohibited to attribute evil to God?
A) Because God cannot create evil
B) Because it might imply God is evil
C) Because evil does not exist
D) Because evil is only in the mind

Key Takeaway
God's creation of evil does not make Him evil; only acquiring evil does.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience or witness evil, do you ever struggle with thoughts that God is somehow tainted by it? How does this lesson's distinction between creation and acquisition challenge you to correct that thought? This week, when such a thought arises, will you remind yourself that God creates but does not acquire evil?
?
Are there areas in your life where you have attributed evil to God—perhaps in hardship or loss—without realizing the adab required? How can you train your heart to see God's creation of all things, including trials, without attributing evil to His character? Write down one practical step you will take to guard your tongue and heart when facing difficulty.
?
If only acquiring evil makes one evil, then your own actions are what define your moral state. How seriously do you take the evil you choose to acquire through your own kasb? Will you commit today to examining one habit or action that you have been excusing, and repent from it, knowing that you—not God—are responsible for your own evil?

The Nature of Evil as Non-Existent
Argues that evil is non-existent and thus not created

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the view that evil is essentially non-existent and how this resolves the problem of God creating evil.

Core Idea
Evil is non-existent, so it is not created, and God is not its creator.

This lesson delves into a deeper theological analysis: some scholars argue that evil has no real existence, being merely the absence of good. This eliminates the problem of God creating evil altogether, since non-existence cannot be created.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on evil's non-existence
Read the second part where some scholars argue evil is non-existent.
2
Understand the reasoning
Evil reduces to the non-existence of something. Therefore it is not a real entity.
This means evil is not created.
3
See how it solves the problem
If evil is not created, then God is not the creator of evil, avoiding the implication that He is evil.
The passage also reiterates the kasb argument.

?

?

The Impossibility of Inability in Allah
Defines inability and proves its impossibility through a dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-‘ajz (inability) and the rational proof demonstrating that it cannot be attributed to Allah.

Core Idea
Inability cannot be eternal or originated—both lead to contradictions, so Allah cannot have it.

This lesson explores the attribute of inability (al-‘ajz) and why it is impossible for Allah. The author first defines inability, then presents a disjunctive argument showing that if it were eternal or originated, it would lead to absurdities. This is a classic kalam argument establishing a negative divine attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the compact style and the logical structure.
2
Identify the definition and the proof
The passage first defines 'ajz and then argues that it is impossible for Allah. Find where the definition ends and the proof begins.
3
Trace the disjunctive argument
The proof splits into two possibilities: inability being eternal or originated. Follow each branch and note the absurd conclusions. See how both lead to the same contradiction.
This is a classic 'dilemma' argument in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحقيقة العجز تعذر إيجاد ما يمكن إيجاده وإعدام ما يمكن إعدامه، وهو ضد القدرة، والقدرة واجبة له تعالى والعجز مستحيل على الله فيستحيل على الله العجز عنه لأنه لو اتصف تعالى بالعجز لا يخلو إما أن يكون قديماً أو حادثاً، فإن كان قديما لزم أن لا يعدم لاستحالة عدم القديم. فلا يوصف المولى تبارك وتعالى بالقدرة لاستحالة جمع الضدين. وإذا لم يتصف بالقدرة فينتفي العالم، وهذا محال بالمشاهدة والعيان وإن كان حادثا فإن اتصف به مع القدرة لزم الجمع بين الضدين وإن اتصف به مع عدمها لزم حدوثها لأن كل من يطرأ عليه العدم حادث ، ويلزم من حدوث الصفة حدوث الموصوف فينتفي العالم ونفى العالم محال بالمشاهدة.
The reality of inability (al-‘ajz) is the prevention of bringing into existence what is possible to bring into existence and annihilating what is possible to annihilate. It is the opposite of ability (al-qudrah). Ability is necessary for Him, may He be exalted, and inability is impossible for Allah. Thus inability is impossible for Allah. Because if He, may He be exalted, were described with inability, it would either be eternal or originated. If it is eternal, then it would necessitate that He does not cause things to cease to exist because the cessation of the eternal is impossible. Then the Lord, blessed and exalted, would not be described with ability because of the impossibility of combining two opposites. And if He is not described with ability, then the world would be non-existent, which is impossible by observation and sight. And if it is originated, then if He is described with it along with ability, it necessitates combining two opposites, and if He is described with it without ability, it necessitates its origination, because everything to which non-existence occurs is originated. The origination of the attribute necessitates the origination of the one described, so the world would be non-existent, and the non-existence of the world is impossible by observation.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof. It first defines inability (al-‘ajz) as the inability to create or annihilate what is possible. It then states that inability is the opposite of ability (al-qudrah), which is necessary for Allah, so inability is impossible for Him. The proof proceeds by a dilemma (disjunctive argument): if inability were attributed to Allah, it must be either eternal (qadīm) or originated (ḥādith). If eternal, then it would never cease, meaning Allah could never cause anything to cease existence—otherwise impossibility of ceasing the eternal. Then He could not have ability (since opposites cannot combine), and without ability the world would not exist, which contradicts observable reality. If inability is originated, two subcases: if it exists together with ability, that combines opposites (since ability and inability are opposites); if it exists without ability, then inability is originated, and since an attribute's origination necessitates the origination of the one described (because attributes cannot subsist in something non-existent), Allah would be originated, which is absurd. Both branches lead to the conclusion that the world would not exist, which is false; thus the assumption that inability could be attributed to Allah is impossible.

Key Terms
العجز (al-‘ajz): inability
القدرة (al-qudrah): ability
ضد (ḍidd): opposite
قديم (qadīm): eternal
حادث (ḥādith): originated

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Inability is just a lack of action, not an attribute, so it could be possible for Allah to 'not do' something without being described with inability.
Correction: The passage shows that inability is a real attribute opposite to ability. If Allah could 'not do' something, that would be due to inability, which is impossible because it would lead to contradictions. Allah's not doing something is by choice, not inability.

Checkpoint
What is the logical structure used to prove the impossibility of al-‘ajz for Allah?
A) A direct proof from revelation
B) A disjunctive argument (dilemma) with two possibilities
C) An analogy with human attributes
D) A consensual agreement among scholars

Key Takeaway
Allah cannot be described with inability because both possibilities—eternal or originated—lead to impossible consequences, proving that inability is impossible for Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that Allah is absolutely powerful and cannot be described with any inability, how would that change the way you make dua for things you think are impossible? Will you stop placing limits on what you ask Him for?
?
When you face a situation that feels hopeless, do you ever subconsciously doubt that Allah's power covers it? How does this lesson's proof—that both eternal and originated inability are impossible—challenge that doubt? What will you do the next time hopelessness creeps in?
?
The proof shows that any combination of inability with ability is contradictory. Is there an area of your life where you act as if Allah has both ability and inability—trusting Him for some things but not others? What is one specific area you need to surrender to His absolute power starting today?
